The Considered Similarity and Three Types of Food
The author defines considered similarity and classifies food into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of considered similarity for health preservation and the distinction between actual, remote potential, and close potential food.

Core Idea
Similarity is judged when food becomes part of the organ, not before.

The author begins by setting the foundation for health preservation: the moment when food actually becomes part of the body. He then clarifies that 'food' can mean different things depending on its stage, and he classifies these stages into clear categories. Understanding this classification is key to applying the principle of similarity correctly.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of considered similarity
Read the opening sentence carefully. Notice that similarity is tied to the moment food becomes part of an organ (actual food), not before.
2
Identify the three categories of food
The passage lists three meanings of 'food': actual, remote potential, and close potential. Find each one and note the examples given.
Remote potential: bread, meat. Close potential: humors, secondary moistures.
3
Understand the exclusion of chyme
Some scholars exclude chyme from the definition of food. Why might that be? Reflect on the criteria.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال والشاكلة المعتبرة بين الغذاء والمغتذى) في حفظ الصحة، هي التي تكون حال ما يصير جزءاً من العضو، إذ هو الغذاء بالفعل، وأما قبله فبالقوة على الاختلاف (۳) في القرب والبعد]. فيه إشارة إلى أمرين أحدهما : أن الغذاء قد يطلق على ما هو بالفعل. أعني الجسم الذي ورد على البدن، واستحال إلى الصورة العضوية، وصارت جزءاً منه شبيهاً به، لكن لم يحصل له القوام التام الذي للعضو للقطع ، بأنه لا يقال للأجزاء من العضو أنها غذاء له، وقد يطلق على ما هو بالقوة البعيدة، أعني الجسم الذي من شأنه، إذا ورد على البدن، وانفعل عن حرارته أن يستحيل إلى الغذاء بالفعل كالخبز واللحم أو القريبة أعني الجسم المعد في البدن لأن يصير غذاء بالفعل كالأخلاط وبعض الرطوبات الثانية (4) ، أعني التي تستحيل إليها الأخلاط، وهل تطلق على الكيلوس منعه بعضهم . وثانيهما: أن المراد بالشاكلة في قولهم حفظ الصحة تكون بالمشاكل، كما أن علاج المريض يكون بالمضاد، موافقة مزاج الغذاء، حين ما هو غذاء بالفعل لمزاج المغتذي، حتى أن غذاء صاحب المزاج الحار ينبغي أن يكون بارداً ، بحيث إذا تصرف فيه طبيعته، فصار غذاء بالفعل، استحال عن البرد، وصار حاراً مشاكلاً لجوهر بدنه، لا أن يكون حاراً مثل مزاجه، وإلا لصار عند الهضم أحر مما ينبغي، وأسقمه، وربما صار من
He said: 'And the considered similarity between food and the nourished body in preserving health is that which occurs at the moment it becomes a part of the organ, for it is food in actuality. Before that, it is potential, with difference in proximity and distance.' In it there is an indication to two matters: First, that 'food' may be applied to that which is actual, namely the body that came to the body and changed into the organic form and became a part of it resembling it, but did not obtain the complete consistency of the organ because it is not said of the parts of the organ that they are food for it. And it may be applied to that which is remote potential, namely the body that, when it comes to the body and is acted upon by its heat, changes into actual food, like bread and meat; or close potential, namely the body prepared in the body to become actual food, like the humors and some secondary moistures, namely those into which the humors change. And some prevented applying it to chyme. Second, that by 'similarity' in their saying 'health preservation is by similarity, just as treatment of the sick is by opposition,' is the agreement of the temperament of the food at the time it is actual food with the temperament of the nourished, so that the food of a hot-tempered person should be cold, so that when his nature acts on it and it becomes actual food, it changes from cold to hot, matching his body's substance, not that it be hot like his temperament, otherwise at digestion it would become hotter than appropriate and make him ill, and perhaps it would become from
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage discusses the concept of 'considered similarity' (الشاكلة المعتبرة) for health preservation. The author states that similarity should be considered at the moment food becomes part of the organ (actual food), not before. He then makes two points. First: 'food' has three meanings: (1) actual food — the transformed part of an organ (but not the organ itself), (2) remote potential — bread, meat that will transform, (3) close potential — humors and secondary moistures already in the body. Some exclude chyme. Second: for health, the food's temperament when it is actual food should match the body's temperament. For a hot-tempered person, food should initially be cold so that, through digestion, it becomes warm and matches the body. If it were already hot, it would become excessively hot and cause harm.

Key Terms
شاكلة
حفظ الصحة
غذاء بالفعل
غذاء بالقوة
مشاكل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think similarity should be assessed at the time of eating or before digestion.
Correction: The author specifies that similarity is considered at the moment the food becomes part of the organ (actual food), not at earlier stages.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of remote potential food?
A) Humors
B) Bread and meat
C) Secondary moistures
D) A part of the organ

Key Takeaway
Similarity for health preservation is judged only when food becomes actual part of the organ, and food can be actual, remote potential, or close potential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You eat bread daily. Has it ever occurred to you that until that bread becomes a literal part of your cells, it is not actually nourishing you? What does that reveal about the distance between what you 'consume' spiritually and what truly becomes part of your character?
?
The author says food is only considered similar when it has become part of the organ. Is there a truth you have heard many times but never allowed to become part of you? What would it take for that truth to actually nourish your soul?
?
Some scholars refused to call chyme 'food' because it had not yet fully transformed. Are there beliefs or habits in your life that are like chyme—inside you but not yet integrated? Will you leave them as half-digested or complete the transformation?

Learning Objective

For health, food should match the body's temperament after digestion, not before.

Having defined similarity and the types of food, the author now explains how similarity is actually employed in preserving health. He contrasts it with treatment by opposites and provides a concrete example involving a hot-tempered person. This practical illustration shows why food must be chosen with the digestive process in mind.

The Objection and the Appropriate Similarity (الشاكلة)
Author responds to an objection and defines الشاكلة

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of الشاكلة (appropriate similarity) resolves the objection about food temperament during health preservation.

Core Idea
The appropriate similarity is determined when food becomes part of the organ.

Before defining health preservation by similars, the author addresses a potential objection: how can food match the organ's temperament if it contains excess moisture? He offers a counter-explanation involving coagulation, then provides the key definition of الشاكلة as the similarity at the moment food becomes actual part of the body. This lesson establishes the conceptual foundation for the practical rules that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice how the objector claims food cannot be on the organ's temperament due to excess moisture, and the author's reply that transformation can occur by coagulation, not just dissolution.
2
Identify the definition of الشاكلة
Find where the author defines the 'appropriate similarity' (الشاكلة المعتبرة) and note its key condition: it is considered when the food becomes part of the organ (actual food), not before.
This is a crucial shift from the objection.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider: how does this definition resolve the objection? The objection assumed transformation requires dissolution of moisture; the reply provides an alternative (coagulation). The definition then clarifies that similarity is measured at the moment of incorporation.
This sets the stage for the practical rule in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض بأنها حينئذ لا تكون على مزاج العضو لما فيها من زيادة مائية لا بد من تحللها. ورد بأنه يجوز أن تكون الاستحالة إلى قوام العضو لا بتحلل المائية، بل بمجرد الانعقاد كاللحم يتولد من منتن الدم، ويعقده الحر، والشحم من مائيته ودسميته، ويعقده البرد. [قال والشاكلة المعتبرة بين الغذاء والمغتذى في حفظ الصحة، هي التي تكون حال ما يصير جزءاً من العضو، إذ هو الغذاء بالفعل، وأما قبله فبالقوة على الاختلاف في القرب والبعد]. الكاملة فيه إشارة إلى أمرين أحدهما: أن الغذاء قد يطلق على ما هو بالفعل. أعني الجسم الذي ورد على البدن، واستحال إلى الصورة العضوية، وصارت جزءاً منه شبيهاً به، لكن لم يحصل له القوام التام الذي للعضو للقطع بأنه لا يقال للأجزاء من العضو أنها غذاء له، وقد يطلق على ما هو بالقوة البعيدة، أعني الجسم الذي من شأنه، إذا ورد على البدن، وانفعل عن حرارته أن يستحيل إلى الغذاء بالفعل كالخبز واللحم أو القريبة أعني الجسم المعد في البدن لأن يصير غذاء بالفعل كالأخلاط وبعض الرطوبات الثانية، أعني التي تستحيل إليها الأخلاط، وهل تطلق على الكيلوس منعه بعضهم. وثانيهما: أن المراد بالشاكلة في قولهم حفظ الصحة تكون بالمشاكل، كما أن علاج المريض يكون بالمضاد، موافقة مزاج الغذاء، حين ما هو غذاء بالفعل لمزاج المغتذي، حتى أن غذاء صاحب المزاج الحار ينبغي أن يكون بارداً، بحيث إذا تصرف فيه طبيعته، فصار غذاء بالفعل، استحال عن البرد، وصار حاراً مشاكلاً لجوهر بدنه، لا أن يكون حاراً مثل مزاجه، وإلا لصار عند الهضم أحر مما ينبغي، وأسقمه، وربما صار من قبيل الأدوية بل السموم، وكذا غذاء بارد المزاج ينبغي أن يكون حاراً، ليصير عند الهضم في بدنه البارد بارداً مثله، وبهذا يندفع الاعتراض، بأنه لو كان حفظ الصحة بالمشاكل لزم أن يكون غذاء من هو حار المزاج جداً بالمسخنات مثل العسل والفلفل، وبارد المزاج بالمبردات وبطلانه ظاهر.
And an objection was raised that then it (the food) would not be on the temperament of the organ because of the excess moisture that must be dissolved. And it was replied that it is permissible that the transformation to the consistency of the organ is not by dissolution of the moisture, but merely by coagulation, like meat generated from putrid blood, coagulated by heat, and fat from its moisture and oiliness, coagulated by cold. [He said: And the appropriate similarity (al-shākila al-muʿtabara) between the food and the fed in preserving health is that which is at the moment it becomes a part of the organ, for it is actual food; but before that, it is potential food, varying in closeness and distance]. The complete [statement] contains an indication of two matters: One of them: that food may be applied to that which is actual, meaning the body that came upon the body and transformed into organic form and became a part of it similar to it, but did not attain the complete consistency of the organ, decisively because it is not said of the parts of the organ that they are food for it; and it may be applied to that which is in remote potentiality, meaning the body whose nature is such that when it comes upon the body and is affected by its heat, it transforms into actual food, like bread and meat; or close potentiality, meaning the body prepared in the body to become actual food, like the humors and some secondary fluids, meaning those into which the humors transform; and some have denied that it is applied to chyme. And the second: that what is meant by al-shākila in their saying 'health preservation is by similars, just as treating the sick is by opposites,' is the conformity of the temperament of the food, at the time it is actual food, to the temperament of the fed; so that the food of a person of hot temperament ought to be cold, such that when his nature acts upon it and it becomes actual food, it changes from cold and becomes hot, conforming to the substance of his body—not that it be hot like his temperament, otherwise it would become hotter than proper upon digestion and make him ill, and perhaps it would become of the category of medicines or even poisons. Likewise, the food of a person of cold temperament ought to be hot, so that upon digestion in his cold body it becomes cold like it. And by this the objection is refuted that if health preservation were by similars, then the food of a very hot-tempered person would have to be by heating things like honey and pepper, and of a cold-tempered person by cooling things—and its falsehood is apparent.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage begins with an objection and response about food temperament, then defines the concept of 'appropriate similarity' (الشاكلة المعتبرة) in health preservation. The author clarifies that the similarity between food and the body's temperament is considered at the moment the food becomes an actual part of the organ, not before. He then explains two matters: first, the different senses of 'food' (غذاء)—actual food that has already become part of the organ, potential food in remote sense (like bread), and potential food in close sense (like humors). Second, he explains that health preservation requires food to be opposite in temperament to the body at the time of consumption so that after digestion it becomes similar, thus avoiding the objection that heating foods would be prescribed for hot-tempered individuals.

Key Terms
الشاكلة
المزاج
القوام
الغذاء بالفعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that health preservation by similars means the food should have the same temperament as the body at the moment of eating.
Correction: The author clarifies that the appropriate similarity is considered at the moment food becomes part of the organ, not before. Food may be opposite initially so that after digestion it becomes similar.

B) When the food is still potential
C) At the moment food becomes a part of the organ
D) When the food is first ingested

Key Takeaway
The appropriate similarity in health preservation is defined at the moment food becomes actual part of the organ, not before.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that the food you eat only becomes 'actual' when it becomes part of your organ, and its similarity to you is judged at that moment. What does this mean for the way you choose what to eat? Will you continue to prioritize taste and immediate pleasure, or will you begin to ask: 'Will this transform properly into me?' Commit to one change in your diet this week based on this understanding.
?
The author shows that transformation can happen through unexpected paths—coagulation, not just dissolution. Is there a area in your spiritual or physical life where you have been assuming only one method of change is valid? Identify one assumption you hold about how you must improve (e.g., 'I must eliminate bad habits completely before I can grow') and deliberately try a different path this week.
?
Before incorporation, food is only potentially beneficial. You likely consume many things—knowledge, relationships, experiences—that remain merely potential, never becoming actual parts of you. Which one 'potential' in your life have you been leaving unrealized? What is the specific step you will take today to make it actual?

The Two Meanings of 'Food' and the Rule of Health Preservation
Author classifies food types and applies الشاكلة to health preservation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of food into actual and potential types, and how the concept of الشاكلة implies that health preservation uses opposites at consumption to achieve similars after digestion.

Core Idea
Health preservation requires food to be opposite initially but similar after digestion.

The author expands on the definition of الشاكلة by first clarifying the different senses in which 'food' (غذاء) is used: actual, potential-remote, and potential-close. Then he explains the practical rule: to preserve health, one should consume food of opposite temperament so that after digestion it becomes similar to the body's temperament, refuting the idea that heating foods should be given to hot-tempered persons. This lesson applies the earlier definition to the concrete practice of dietary recommendation.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of food
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Note the three categories: actual food (already part of organ), potential-remote (like bread), and potential-close (like humors). Understand that only actual food is considered for similarity.
2
Understand the rule for health preservation
The author then states that health preservation by similars means the food's temperament at the time of actual food should conform to the body's temperament. But that requires the food to be opposite initially so that digestion changes it to match. Look for the example of hot and cold temperaments.
This is the key practical takeaway.
3
Refute the objection
The passage ends by refuting a potential misunderstanding: that preservation by similars would require giving heating foods to hot-tempered persons. The author shows that this is false because the initial food must be opposite.
Connect this to the earlier objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض بأنها حينئذ لا تكون على مزاج العضو لما فيها من زيادة مائية لا بد من تحللها. ورد بأنه يجوز أن تكون الاستحالة إلى قوام العضو لا بتحلل المائية، بل بمجرد الانعقاد كاللحم يتولد من منتن الدم، ويعقده الحر، والشحم من مائيته ودسميته، ويعقده البرد. [قال والشاكلة المعتبرة بين الغذاء والمغتذى في حفظ الصحة، هي التي تكون حال ما يصير جزءاً من العضو، إذ هو الغذاء بالفعل، وأما قبله فبالقوة على الاختلاف في القرب والبعد]. الكاملة فيه إشارة إلى أمرين أحدهما: أن الغذاء قد يطلق على ما هو بالفعل. أعني الجسم الذي ورد على البدن، واستحال إلى الصورة العضوية، وصارت جزءاً منه شبيهاً به، لكن لم يحصل له القوام التام الذي للعضو للقطع بأنه لا يقال للأجزاء من العضو أنها غذاء له، وقد يطلق على ما هو بالقوة البعيدة، أعني الجسم الذي من شأنه، إذا ورد على البدن، وانفعل عن حرارته أن يستحيل إلى الغذاء بالفعل كالخبز واللحم أو القريبة أعني الجسم المعد في البدن لأن يصير غذاء بالفعل كالأخلاط وبعض الرطوبات الثانية، أعني التي تستحيل إليها الأخلاط، وهل تطلق على الكيلوس منعه بعضهم. وثانيهما: أن المراد بالشاكلة في قولهم حفظ الصحة تكون بالمشاكل، كما أن علاج المريض يكون بالمضاد، موافقة مزاج الغذاء، حين ما هو غذاء بالفعل لمزاج المغتذي، حتى أن غذاء صاحب المزاج الحار ينبغي أن يكون بارداً، بحيث إذا تصرف فيه طبيعته، فصار غذاء بالفعل، استحال عن البرد، وصار حاراً مشاكلاً لجوهر بدنه، لا أن يكون حاراً مثل مزاجه، وإلا لصار عند الهضم أحر مما ينبغي، وأسقمه، وربما صار من قبيل الأدوية بل السموم، وكذا غذاء بارد المزاج ينبغي أن يكون حاراً، ليصير عند الهضم في بدنه البارد بارداً مثله، وبهذا يندفع الاعتراض، بأنه لو كان حفظ الصحة بالمشاكل لزم أن يكون غذاء من هو حار المزاج جداً بالمسخنات مثل العسل والفلفل، وبارد المزاج بالمبردات وبطلانه ظاهر.
And an objection was raised that then it (the food) would not be on the temperament of the organ because of the excess moisture that must be dissolved. And it was replied that it is permissible that the transformation to the consistency of the organ is not by dissolution of the moisture, but merely by coagulation, like meat generated from putrid blood, coagulated by heat, and fat from its moisture and oiliness, coagulated by cold. [He said: And the appropriate similarity (al-shākila al-muʿtabara) between the food and the fed in preserving health is that which is at the moment it becomes a part of the organ, for it is actual food; but before that, it is potential food, varying in closeness and distance]. The complete [statement] contains an indication of two matters: One of them: that food may be applied to that which is actual, meaning the body that came upon the body and transformed into organic form and became a part of it similar to it, but did not attain the complete consistency of the organ, decisively because it is not said of the parts of the organ that they are food for it; and it may be applied to that which is in remote potentiality, meaning the body whose nature is such that when it comes upon the body and is affected by its heat, it transforms into actual food, like bread and meat; or close potentiality, meaning the body prepared in the body to become actual food, like the humors and some secondary fluids, meaning those into which the humors transform; and some have denied that it is applied to chyme. And the second: that what is meant by al-shākila in their saying 'health preservation is by similars, just as treating the sick is by opposites,' is the conformity of the temperament of the food, at the time it is actual food, to the temperament of the fed; so that the food of a person of hot temperament ought to be cold, such that when his nature acts upon it and it becomes actual food, it changes from cold and becomes hot, conforming to the substance of his body—not that it be hot like his temperament, otherwise it would become hotter than proper upon digestion and make him ill, and perhaps it would become of the category of medicines or even poisons. Likewise, the food of a person of cold temperament ought to be hot, so that upon digestion in his cold body it becomes cold like it. And by this the objection is refuted that if health preservation were by similars, then the food of a very hot-tempered person would have to be by heating things like honey and pepper, and of a cold-tempered person by cooling things—and its falsehood is apparent.
Source pages p. 250

Commentary
This passage begins with an objection and response about food temperament, then defines the concept of 'appropriate similarity' (الشاكلة المعتبرة) in health preservation. The author clarifies that the similarity between food and the body's temperament is considered at the moment the food becomes an actual part of the organ, not before. He then explains two matters: first, the different senses of 'food' (غذاء)—actual food that has already become part of the organ, potential food in remote sense (like bread), and potential food in close sense (like humors). Second, he explains that health preservation requires food to be opposite in temperament to the body at the time of consumption so that after digestion it becomes similar, thus avoiding the objection that heating foods would be prescribed for hot-tempered individuals.

Key Terms
الغذاء بالفعل
الغذاء بالقوة
الخلط
الكيلوس
المشاكل
المضاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think health preservation by similars means eating foods of the same temperament as one's body.
Correction: The author clarifies that the food should be opposite so that after digestion it becomes similar, avoiding overheating or overcooling.

Checkpoint

A) Hot food
B) Cold food
C) Food of neutral temperature
D) Any food as long as it is similar after digestion

Key Takeaway
Health preservation requires initially opposite food so that after digestion it becomes similar to the body's temperament, preventing harm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson teaches that true health comes from foods that transform to match your body's temperament after digestion, not from what initially seems good. Where in your life have you been pursuing immediate pleasure or comfort instead of trusting a deeper process of transformation? What would it look like to choose 'cold' food (a discomfort) today for the sake of long-term balance?
?
The author refutes a common misunderstanding that 'preservation by similars' means eating what is like you. Are there beliefs you hold about your spiritual or physical health that you have never questioned deeply? Will you examine them with the same rigor as this lesson does, even if it means abandoning comfortable assumptions?

Definition of the Vegetative Power
Defines the vegetative power and its relation to the nutritive power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the vegetative power and how it differs from the nutritive power.

Core Idea
Vegetative power causes proportional growth by introducing food into the body.

In this lesson, we examine the foundational definition of one of the natural powers: the vegetative power. The author clarifies what it means for the body to grow and how this power is sometimes identified with the nutritive power, especially in early life. This sets the stage for understanding the precise conditions of genuine growth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and translation carefully. Identify the core definition and the remark about the nutritive power.
Notice the emphasis on 'natural proportion'.
2
Understand the definition
The power introduces food between parts and increases dimensions proportionally. Consider what 'proportionally' means here.
3
Consider life stages
The passage mentions that early in life this power suffices for both replacement and increase, but later only for replacement. Why is that?
Refer to the commentary about excess power, small body, abundant moisture.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ومنها) النامية وهي التي تدخل الغذاء بين أجزاء الجسم، فتزيد في أقطاره بنسبة طبيعته، وقد يقال إنها الغاذية، إلا أنها في الابتداء تفي بإيراد البدل والزيادة، لفرط القوة، وصغر الجثة، وكثرة الرطوبة، وفي الآخر تعجز عن ذلك. أي ومن القوى الطبيعية النامية، وهي التي تزيد في أقطار الجسم، أعني الطول والعرض والعمق، على التناسب الطبيعي، بما تدخل في أجزائه من الغذاء، فخرج ما يفيد السمن، لأنه لا يكون زيادة في الطول وفيه نظر، والورم لأنه لا يكون على التناسب الطبيعي. أي النسبة التي تقتضيها طبيعة ذلك الشخص، والتخلخل، لأنه لا يكون بما يدخل في الجسم، بل بانبساط جرمه، وأما التخلخل بمعنى الانتفاش أعني مداخلة الأجزاء الهوائية. فلو سلم تناول الجنس أعني القوة الطبيعية لما يفيده، لخرج بقيد الغذاء، لظهور أن الأجزاء الهوائية ليست غذاء للمتنفس والأكثرون على أن قيد مداخلة الغذاء في أجزاء الجسم، يخرج السمن أيضاً، لأنه لا يدخل في جوهر الأعضاء الأصلية المتولدة من المنى، بل في الأعضاء المتولدة من الدم، ومائيته كاللحم والدم والشحم والسمن.
He said: 'And among them is the vegetative (al-nāmiya), which is that which introduces food between the parts of the body, and increases its dimensions (aqtār) according to its natural proportion (nisba ṭabī‘īya). It may be said that it is the nutritive (al-ghādhiya), except that in the beginning it suffices for the replacement and increase due to the excess of power and smallness of the body (al-juththa) and abundance of moisture, and in the end it becomes incapable of that.' That is, from the natural powers is the vegetative, which is that which increases the dimensions of the body, meaning length, width, and depth, according to natural proportion (al-tanāsub al-ṭabī‘ī), by what enters its parts of food. So there is excluded what produces fat (al-siman), because it is not an increase in length – and there is consideration in that – and tumor (al-waram) because it is not according to natural proportion, i.e. the proportion required by the nature of that person, and rarefaction (al-takhlukhul) because it is not by what enters the body, but by the expansion of its substance. As for rarefaction in the sense of inflation, i.e. the interpenetration of airy parts. If the genus, i.e. the natural power, were admitted for what it yields, it would be excluded by the condition of food, because it is apparent that the airy parts are not food for the breather. And most are of the opinion that the condition of the interpenetration of food into the parts of the body excludes fat as well, because it does not enter into the substance of the original organs generated from semen, but into the organs generated from blood and their moisture, like flesh, blood, and fat (shaḥm) and suet (saman).
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage defines the vegetative power (quwwa nāmiya) as one of the natural powers. It first gives the core definition: it introduces food into the body's parts and increases its dimensions (length, width, depth) according to a natural proportion. It then notes that some identify it with the nutritive power (ghādhiya), but the vegetative power in early life provides both replacement and growth due to the body's small size and abundant moisture, while later it can only replace. The commentary expands: the increase must be by food entering the parts, and the dimensions must grow proportionally. This excludes: (1) fat increase, because it does not involve proportional length growth (though this is debated); (2) tumor, because it is not proportional; (3) rarefaction, because it comes from expansion of the body's substance, not from food; the rarefication from airy parts is also excluded because air is not food. Finally, the condition of food entering the body's parts also excludes fat, because fat does not become part of the original organs from semen but only from blood and its fluids like flesh, blood, and suet.

Key Terms
النامية (quwwa nāmiya)
الغاذية (quwwa ghādhiya)
الأقطار (aqtār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in the beginning the vegetative power...
A) only increases
B) only replaces
C) suffices for both replacement and increase
D) is incapable of both

Key Takeaway
The vegetative power is defined as causing proportional growth by introducing food, and it is distinguished from the nutritive power by its ability to increase growth in early life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are told that in early life the vegetative power suffices for both replacement and increase, but later only replacement. Reflect on your own spiritual growth: are you still in the phase of increase, or have you settled for mere maintenance? What concrete change would prove you are still growing?
?
The vegetative power introduces food between the parts of the body for proportional growth. What 'spiritual food' are you introducing into your life, and is it leading to proportional growth or just maintaining the status quo?
?
The author notes that the vegetative power is often identified with the nutritive power, but they are distinct in function. Are you confusing religious duties that maintain your faith (like routine prayers) with those that cause spiritual growth (like deeper study and self-accountability)? What step can you take today to ensure you are not just maintaining but increasing?

Conditions of Vegetative Growth and Exclusions
Explains conditions and excluded types of increase.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three conditions for genuine vegetative growth and the three types of increase that are excluded: fat, tumor, and rarefaction.

Core Idea
True growth must be proportional, by food, and in all dimensions; fat, tumor, and rarefaction are excluded.

The author now elaborates on the definition of the vegetative power by listing conditions that must be met for an increase to count as genuine growth. He then examines three apparent counterexamples—fat, tumor, and rarefaction—and shows why each fails to meet one or more conditions. This sharpens our understanding of what 'growth' means in this tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions
The passage specifies that the increase must be in length, width, and depth according to natural proportion, and it must come from food entering the parts.
Look for 'على التناسب الطبيعي' and 'بما تدخل في أجزائه من الغذاء'.
2
Examine each exclusion
Fat is excluded because it doesn't increase length; tumor lacks proportion; rarefaction (two senses) is not via food.
Note the debate about fat: some exclude it also because it doesn't integrate into original organs.
3
Consider the debate about fat
Most scholars exclude fat because it does not enter the substance of original organs from semen, only into later tissues from blood.
This is an additional refinement to the food condition.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

What would it take for your faith to grow 'by food entering the original organs' of your soul?

Refuting the Objection on Industrial Increase
Refutes an objection that dimensional condition would include candle reshaping.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the condition of food interpenetration distinguishes natural growth from artificial reshaping, and how the author refutes the candle objection.

Core Idea
Natural growth requires internal penetration of food, not just external reshaping.

In this passage, the author addresses a clever objection: if growth is defined by increase in dimensions, then reshaping a candle (decreasing length but increasing width) would count as growth. This seems to expose a flaw. But the author explains that the discussion is about natural powers, where increase comes specifically from the interpenetration of food into the substance. The objection confuses artificial manipulation with genuine growth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the objection and the author's response.
Notice the author's dismissive phrase 'kalam qalil al-jadwa' (a statement of little benefit).
2
Understand the objection
The Imam argues that the condition of dimensions (al-aqtār) would incorrectly include industrial increases like reshaping a candle: decreasing length increases width, yet this is not natural growth.
3
Grasp the key distinction
The author clarifies that the discourse is about natural powers, where increase comes specifically from the interpenetration (al-mudākhala) of food. Merely attaching extra wax does not constitute growth because it lacks internal penetration.
4
Reflect on the text vs commentary clarification
The author notes that in the main text the condition of interpenetration was introduced from the perspective of existence (wujūd), while in the commentary the condition of increase was from the perspective of manifestation (ẓuhūr). This further shows the objection is irrelevant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكره الإمام من أن قيد الأقطار يخرج الزيادات الصناعية، كما إذا أخذت شمعة وشكلتها بشكل، فإنك متى نقصت من طولها زدت في عرضها كلام قليل الجدوى، لأن الكلام في القوى الطبيعية، وفي أن تكون الزيادة بمداخلة الغذاء وإلا فلاخفاء في أنك إذا ضممت ومزجت بالشمعة قدراً آخر من الشمع، حصلت الزيادة في الأقطار، وإنما قدمنا في المتن قيد المداخلة نظراً إلى الوجود. وفي الشرح قيد الزيادة نظراً إلى الظهور.
And what the Imam mentioned – that the condition of dimensions (al-aqtār) excludes industrial increases, as when you take a candle and shape it in a form, for whenever you decrease its length you increase its width – is a statement of little benefit. Because the discourse is about natural powers, and that the increase is by the interpenetration (al-mudākhala) of food. Otherwise, it is not hidden that if you attach and mix with the candle another amount of wax, the increase in dimensions would occur. And we only introduced in the text the condition of interpenetration from the perspective of existence (al-wujūd), and in the commentary the condition of increase from the perspective of manifestation (al-ẓuhūr).
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection attributed to 'the Imam' (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi or Imam al-Haramayn). The objector argued that the condition of 'dimensions' (aqtār) in the definition of vegetative growth would exclude industrial increases like reshaping a candle: decreasing length while increasing width still changes dimensions, yet this is not natural growth. The author dismisses this as 'kalam qalil al-jadwa' (a statement of little benefit). The explanation follows: the discussion concerns natural powers, where increase comes specifically from the interpenetration (mudākhala) of food into the body's parts. If you simply attach extra wax to a candle (without mixing it into the substance), that would indeed yield an increase in dimensions, but that is not the point—the required increase is through food interpenetration. The author clarifies that in the main text they introduced the condition of interpenetration with an eye to actual existence (wujūd), while in the commentary they introduced the condition of increase with an eye to manifest appearance (ẓuhūr). Thus, the objection does not affect the definition because it confuses natural growth with artificial reshaping.

Key Terms
الأقطار (al-aqtār)
الزيادات الصناعية (al-ziyādāt al-ṣinā‘iyya)
المداخلة (al-mudākhala)
الوجود (al-wujūd)
الظهور (al-ẓuhūr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any increase in dimensions, even through artificial reshaping, counts as growth.
Correction: Natural growth requires increase through interpenetration of food into the substance, not merely external manipulation.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the Imam's objection about the candle to be 'kalam qalil al-jadwa' (of little benefit)?
A) Because the Imam misunderstood the definition of dimensions.
B) Because the objection confuses natural growth with artificial reshaping; the real condition is food interpenetration.
C) Because the candle example involves a decrease in length, not an increase.
D) Because the Imam did not accept the existence of natural powers.

Key Takeaway
Natural growth is defined by increase through internal food interpenetration, not merely by change in dimensions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your spiritual practice, are you merely rearranging outward acts (like a reshaped candle) or allowing the ‘food’ of divine guidance to penetrate your inner self? What would it take to let that penetration happen today?
?
In your pursuit of knowledge, do you measure growth by how much you acquire or by how deeply it transforms you? Will you commit to letting one truth from this lesson penetrate your heart and change how you live?
?
Are you content with a change in dimensions—more Quran memorized, more prayers performed—without the internal transformation that comes from sincere reflection? What step will you take this week to move from reshaping to real growth?

Defining Natural Growth: Food Integration vs. Artificial Increase
Author defines growth and refutes objections from al-Razi and Ibn Sina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of natural growth (al-namw) as increase through internal integration of food, and how to distinguish it from mere artificial addition or shaping.

Core Idea
True growth is not just getting bigger – it requires the integration of food into the very substance of the body.

This passage tackles a foundational question in natural philosophy: what counts as genuine growth? The author responds to two objections—one from Imam al-Razi about artificial shaping, another from Ibn Sina about pain—to clarify that growth is a specific natural process involving the internal assimilation of food, not mere external addition. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the concept of the 'nutritive faculty' (al-quwwa al-nāmiya) in Islamic philosophical theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the English translation. Pay attention to the flow of objection and reply.
This passage is philosophically dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the author's definition of growth
Find where the author defines growth (al-namw) as 'making the body greater than it was in the specific manner'. Note the key condition of internal integration of food (madākhalat al-ghidhā').
The definition is at the end of the passage.
3
Examine Imam al-Razi's objection and the reply
Al-Razi objects that the restriction of dimensions excludes artificial shaping (like molding a candle). The author replies that the discussion concerns natural powers, not manual addition.
This clarifies that growth must involve internal integration, not just any size increase.
4
Understand the linguistic nuance of 'al-nāmiya'
The author explains that calling the power 'al-nāmiya' is a linguistic extension; the term 'growing' (al-nāmī) properly belongs to the body that receives the food incrementally.
This resolves a potential confusion about the active vs. passive sense.
5
Consider Ibn Sina's objection and the answer
Ibn Sina argues that if food enters pores and separates parts, it would cause pain. The author replies that the separation is natural and painless, unlike violent tearing.
This reaffirms that growth is a gentle, natural process.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشحم والسمن وما ذكره الإمام من أن قيد الأقطار يخرج الزيادات الصناعية، كما إذا أخذت شمعة وشكلتها بشكل، فإنك متى نقصت من طولها زدت في عرضها كلام قليل الجدوى، لأن الكلام في القوى الطبيعية، وفي أن تكون الزيادة بمداخلة الغذاء وإلا فلاخفاء في أنك إذا ضممت ومزجت بالشمعة قدراً آخر من الشمع، حصلت الزيادة في الأقطار، وإنما قدمنا في المتن قيد المداخلة نظراً إلى الوجود. وفي الشرح قيد الزيادة نظراً إلى الظهور، ولا يخفى أن إطلاق النامية على القوة بالنظر إلى الوضع اللغوي من قبيل سيل مفعم على لفظ اسم المفعول، وذلك لأن فعلها، إنما هو الإنماء والنامي. إنما هو الجسم قبل الزيادة التي بها يحصل النمو، وليست في الجسم الأصلي، ولا الوارد، لأن كلاً منهما على حاله، فإذن كل منهما كما كان، وإنما انضاف جسم إلى جسم، فصار المجموع أعظم من كل منهما، وهذا المجموع لم يكن قبل ذلك صغيراً ثم عظم. فإذن ليس ههنا جسم نام. المقدمة الأولى على ما قال له ابن سينا: إذ أن القوة النامية وأجيب: بمنع تفرق أجزاء الجسم، بل اتصال العضو، وتدخل في تلك المسام الأجزاء الغذائية، ولا يلزمه الإيلام، لأن ذلك إنما هو في التفريق الغير الطبيعي. وبالجملة. لما كان معنى النمو، صيرورة الجسم أعظم مما كان بالطريق المخصوص، كان النامي ذلك الجسم، الذي ورد عليه الغذاء، وهو في أول الأمر الجسم الأصلي، ثم الحاصل بالتغذية والتنمية، وهكذا إلى أن يبلغ كمال النشوء.
And [concerning] fat and clarified butter and what the Imam mentioned that the restriction of dimensions excludes artificial increases, such as when you take a candle and shape it into a form: whenever you decrease its length you increase its width – talk of little benefit, because the discussion is about natural powers and about increase through food integration; otherwise it is obvious that if you join and mix with the candle another amount of wax, the increase in dimensions occurs. And we only introduced in the text the condition of integration with respect to existence, and in the commentary the condition of increase with respect to manifestation. And it is not hidden that applying (the term) 'al-nāmiyah' to the power, according to linguistic usage, is of the type of a [؟] overflowing upon the form of the passive participle, because its action is only causing growth (inmā'), while 'the growing thing' (al-nāmī) is the body before the increase by which growth occurs, and it is neither in the original body nor in the incoming (food), because each of them is as it was. So then each of them is as it was, and only a body has been added to a body, so the sum becomes greater than each of them, and this sum was not before that small and then became large. So then there is here no growing body. The first premise according to what Ibn Sīnā said to him: that the growth power [is...] and it was answered: by preventing the separation of the parts of the body, but rather the connection of the limb, and the food particles enter into those pores, and pain does not follow necessarily, because that is only in unnatural separation. In summary: since the meaning of growth (al-namū) is making the body greater than it was in the specific manner, the growing thing is that body which receives the food, and it is in the first instance the original body, then what results from nutrition and growth, and so on until it reaches the perfection of development.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage defines natural growth and refutes objections. The author begins by mentioning an objection from Imam al-Razi about artificial increase (like shaping a candle) not being considered growth because the restriction of dimensions excludes it. The author replies that the discussion is about natural powers, where increase must come through food integration, not mere addition of external matter. He then explains that linguistic usage of 'al-nāmiyah' for the power is figurative; the growing body is the original body that sequentially integrates food. An objection from Ibn Sina about the growth power causing pain is answered by stating that natural integration does not involve painful separation. The passage concludes with a precise definition: growth is making the body larger in a specific natural manner through successive nutrition and development.

Key Terms
القوة النامية
النمو
المداخلة
الأقطار
الغذاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Growth is any increase in size, including artificial addition or reshaping (like adding wax to a candle).
Correction: True growth requires internal integration of food into the substance of the body, not just external addition.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what distinguishes natural growth from artificial increase?
A) Natural growth involves increase in dimensions only.
B) Natural growth requires integration of food into the body's substance.
C) Natural growth is any increase in size, whether by addition or shaping.
D) Natural growth only occurs in plants, not animals.

Key Takeaway
Genuine natural growth is not merely an increase in size; it is a process of internal assimilation of food, painless and continuous, that makes the body greater in a specific manner.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that true growth requires internal integration, not just external addition. In your own life, are you merely adding religious practices (extra prayers, fasting) without allowing them to transform your inner self? What one practice can you focus on assimilating until it becomes part of your character?
?
The author dismisses artificial increase as irrelevant to natural growth. How much of your knowledge about Islam is merely accumulated—like wax added to a candle—versus truly integrated into your heart and actions? What is one piece of knowledge you have studied but not yet lived?
?
Ibn Sina's objection assumes that change must be painful, but the author shows natural growth is painless. Where in your spiritual journey have you resisted change because you feared discomfort, when in fact Allah's way is gentle and natural? Will you trust His process and allow the food of guidance to enter your soul without resistance?

Distinction Between Nutritive and Growth Faculties
The author explains the difference between the nutritive and growth faculties based on Ibn Sina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the nutritive and growth faculties differ in function according to Ibn Sina, and why they cannot be reduced to a single power.

Core Idea
Nutrition replaces; growth elongates — they are distinct actions.

In this passage from Sharh al-Maqasid, the author draws on Ibn Sina's al-Shifa to clarify a subtle distinction between two bodily faculties. While both involve bringing food to limbs, their effects are fundamentally different: one maintains equality, the other creates asymmetry. Understanding this distinction is key to grasping the broader Islamic philosophical account of living beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the full Arabic passage once, then the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally before analyzing it.
The passage contains an objection and a rebuttal; notice how the author structures the discussion.
2
Identify the two faculties
Find where the nutritive faculty (al-ghādhiya) and growth faculty (al-nāmiya) are defined. Note how each is described in terms of bringing food and attaching it.
Their similarity is highlighted at first, then the key difference emerges.
3
Trace the objection and answer
Look for the objection that the nutritive faculty could also do what the growth does, and the author's response about initial strength and later decline.
The objection is based on the idea that ability to do something implies ability to do its like.
4
Study the clarification from al-Shifa
Read the excerpt from Ibn Sina's al-Shifa that explains the difference: the nutritive gives equally, the growth takes from one part and adds to another.
This part distinguishes fattening (width increase) from elongation (length increase).
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters for understanding life and death. The passage ends by linking failure of growth to the arrival of the appointed term.
This connects the biological faculty to a broader theological concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وقد يقال : إشارة إلى ما ذكره الإمام من أن فعل النامية إيراد الغذاء إلى العضو، وتشبيهه به وإلصاقه كالغازية، إلا أن الغازية تفعل هذه الأفعال، بحيث يكون الوارد مساوياً للمتحلل والنامية تفعل أزيد من المتحلل. ولا شك أن القادر على الشيء، قادر على مثله، والجزء الزائد مشابه للأصل، فإذا قويت الغاذية على تحصيل الأصل، قويت على تحصيل الزائد، وتكون هي النامية، إلا أنها في الابتداء تكون قوية على إيراد بدل الأصل والزائد معاً، لشدة القوة على الفعل، وكثرة المادة أعني الرطوبة وقلة الحاجة بواسطة صغر العضو، وبعد ذلك يعود الأمر إلى النقصان لضعف في القوة وقلة في المادة، وعظم في العضو. واعترض بأن التغذية والتنمية فعلان مختلفان فلا يستندان إلى مبدأ واحد حتى أن أمر التغذية لما كان بإيراد البدل والشبيه والالصاق أسندوه إلى قوى ثلاث، وهذا ما قال في الشفاء. إن شأن الغازية أن تؤتي كل عضو من الغذاء بقدر عظمه وصغره، وتلصق به من الغذاء بمقداره الذي له على السواء. وأما النامية فتسلب جانباً من البدن من الغذاء ما يحتاج إليه لزيادة في جهة أخرى، فتلصقه بتلك الجهة لتزيد تلك الجهة فوق زيادة جهة أخرى. بيان ذلك : أن الغاذية إذا انفردت وقوي فعلها، وكان ما تورد أكثر مما يتحلل، فإنها تزيد في عرض الأعضاء وعمقها زيادة ظاهرة بالتسمين، ولا تزيد في الطول زيادة يعتد بها، والنامية تزيد في الطول أكثر كثيراً مما تزيد في العرض. قال ولهذا لما أدى الضعف وقلة الرطوبة إلى العجز عن إيراد البدن حل الأجل.
His saying 'And it may be said' is a reference to what the Imam mentioned, that the action of the growth faculty is bringing food to the limb, making it similar to it, and attaching it like the nutritive faculty, except that the nutritive faculty performs these actions such that the incoming (food) equals the decomposed matter, while the growth faculty does more than the decomposed matter. And there is no doubt that the one who is able to do something is able to do its like, and the extra part resembles the original. So if the nutritive faculty is able to obtain the original, it is able to obtain the extra, and it would be the growth faculty, except that at the beginning it is able to bring both the replacement of the original and the extra together, due to the strength of the power to act, the abundance of matter (i.e., moisture), and the small need due to the smallness of the limb. After that, the situation returns to deficiency due to weakness in the power, scarcity of matter, and large size of the limb. And it was objected that nutrition and growth are two different actions, so they cannot be attributed to a single principle, so much so that since the affair of nutrition is by bringing the replacement, the similar, and attachment, they attributed it to three powers. And this is what was said in al-Shifā': The function of the nutritive faculty is to give each limb of the food according to its size and smallness, and to attach to it from the food exactly the amount that it needs equally. As for the growth faculty, it takes from one side of the body from the food what it needs for increase in another direction, and attaches it to that direction to make that direction increase beyond the increase of another direction. Explanation of that: When the nutritive faculty operates alone and its action is strong, and what it brings is more than what decomposes, it increases the width and depth of the limbs a manifest increase by fattening, and it does not increase the length by a noticeable increase. The growth faculty increases the length much more than it increases the width. He said: And for this reason, when weakness and lack of moisture lead to inability to bring (food) to the body, the appointed term (of life) arrives.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between the nutritive faculty (al-ghādhiya) and the growth faculty (al-nāmiya). The author begins by summarizing an earlier view: both faculties bring food, attach it, and make it similar, but the nutritive replaces exactly what is lost, while the growth adds extra. An objection is raised: if the nutritive can obtain the original, it can obtain the extra, so the same power should suffice. The author replies that initially the faculty is strong enough to do both, but later weakness and scarcity of moisture cause decline. However, the objection continues that nutrition and growth are different actions and cannot come from a single principle; indeed nutrition is attributed to three powers (as in Ibn Sina's 'al-Shifā'). The author then explains the specific distinction: the nutritive faculty gives each limb exactly what it needs equally, while the growth faculty takes from one part and adds to another, causing increase in length more than width. When the nutritive acts alone with excess, it causes fattening (increase in width and depth), but the growth faculty causes elongation. Finally, when weakness and lack of moisture prevent this process, death occurs.

Key Terms
الغاذية
النامية
المتحلل
التسمين
الشفاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The nutritive faculty and growth faculty are essentially the same power because both bring food to limbs.
Correction: They are distinct: the nutritive replaces exactly what is lost (maintains equality), while the growth adds extra and creates asymmetry, especially in length. Their actions are different and cannot be reduced to a single principle.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between the nutritive faculty and the growth faculty?
A) The nutritive faculty only works in plants, the growth faculty in animals.
B) The nutritive faculty replaces equal amounts, the growth faculty adds extra, especially in length.
C) The nutritive faculty adds extra, the growth faculty replaces losses.
D) Both faculties do the same thing but at different times.

Key Takeaway
The nutritive faculty maintains balance by replacing exactly what is lost, while the growth faculty creates increase, especially in length, and this distinction is crucial for understanding bodily development and the end of life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body is sustained by a precise process of replacement and growth, yet you rarely pause to thank the One who designed it. What would change in your daily routine if you consciously remembered that every breath and every cell is a trust from Allah?
?
The passage ties the inability to bring food to the arrival of the appointed term. Are you living as if death is a distant possibility or a certainty that could come at any moment? What one habit would you change if you truly believed your time is limited?
?
The growth faculty adds asymmetrically, prioritizing elongation over width. In your spiritual life, are you growing in depth and length—in knowledge, sincerity, and action—or are you merely maintaining a comfortable status quo? What concrete step will you take today to seek real growth in your relationship with Allah?

Nutritive Faculty's Potential for Growth
Initial description and argument for nutritive as growth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial argument that the nutritive faculty, if strong enough, could perform the function of growth.

Core Idea
If nutritive can replace the original, it should also be able to add more.

This lesson introduces the discussion on the growth faculty. The author notes that the growth faculty's act resembles the nutritive faculty's, except that nutritive replaces exactly what is lost while growth adds more. He then argues that if the nutritive faculty can produce the original, it should also be able to produce the additional part, implying that the nutritive faculty could potentially serve as the growth faculty under certain conditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on growth faculty
Read the Arabic passage describing the act of the growth faculty and its comparison to the nutritive faculty.
Notice the key terms: النامية (growth), الغاذية (nutritive), التغذية (nutrition), التنمية (growth).
2
Understand the comparison
The growth faculty brings food, assimilates, and attaches like the nutritive faculty, but the nutritive replaces exactly what is dissolved, while growth adds more.
3
Follow the argument
The author argues that since the nutritive can obtain the original (replacement), and the additional part resembles the original, the nutritive should also be able to obtain the additional, thus becoming the growth faculty.
This argument assumes 'one capable of a thing is capable of its like'.
4
Note the exception
The argument applies only initially when power and moisture are abundant; later, it declines due to weakness and scarcity.
The initial conditions are strong power, abundant moisture, small limb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وقد يقال : إشارة إلى ما ذكره الإمام من أن فعل النامية إيراد الغذاء إلى العضو، وتشبيهه به وإلصاقه كالغازية، إلا أن الغازية تفعل هذه الأفعال، بحيث يكون الوارد مساوياً للمتحلل والنامية تفعل أزيد من المتحلل. ولا شك أن القادر على الشيء، قادر على مثله ، والجزء الزائد مشابه للأصل، فإذا قويت الغاذية على تحصيل الأصل، قويت على تحصيل الزائد، وتكون هي النامية، إلا أنها في الابتداء تكون قوية على إيراد بدل الأصل والزائد معاً، لشدة القوة على الفعل، وكثرة المادة أعني الرطوبة وقلة الحاجة بواسطة صغر العضو، وبعد ذلك يعود الأمر إلى النقصان لضعف في القوة وقلة في المادة، وعظم في العضو. واعترض بأن التغذية والتنمية فعلان مختلفان فلا يستندان إلى مبدأ واحد حتى أن أمر التغذية لما كان بإيراد البدل والشبيه والالصاق أسندوه إلى قوى ثلاث، وهذا ما قال في الشفاء إن شأن الغازية أن تؤتي كل عضو من الغذاء بقدر عظمه وصغره، وتلصق به من الغذاء بمقداره الذي له على السواء. وأما النامية فتسلب جانباً من البدن من الغذاء ما يحتاج إليه لزيادة في جهة أخرى، فتلصقه بتلك الجهة لتزيد تلك الجهة فوق زيادة جهة أخرى. بيان ذلك : أن الغاذية إذا انفردت وقوي فعلها، وكان ما تورد أكثر مما يتحلل، فإنها تزيد في عرض الأعضاء وعمقها زيادة ظاهرة بالتسمين، ولا تزيد في الطول زيادة يعتد بها، والنامية تزيد في الطول أكثر كثيراً مما تزيد في العرض.
For his saying: 'And it may be said' is a reference to what the Imam mentioned, that the act of the growth faculty is to bring food to the limb, assimilate it and attach it like the nutritive faculty, except that the nutritive faculty does these actions such that what is brought equals what is dissolved, while the growth faculty does more than what is dissolved. There is no doubt that one who is capable of a thing is capable of its like, and the additional part resembles the original. So if the nutritive faculty is strong enough to obtain the original, it is strong enough to obtain the additional, and it would be the growth faculty, except that in the beginning it is strong enough to bring both the replacement of the original and the additional together, due to the intensity of power in action, abundance of matter (i.e., moisture) and little need due to the smallness of the limb, and after that the matter returns to decline due to weakness in power, scarcity of matter, and largeness of the limb. And it was objected that nutrition and growth are two different actions, so they cannot be attributed to a single principle; indeed, since the matter of nutrition involves bringing the replacement, the similar, and attachment, they attributed it to three powers. And this is what he said in al-Shifa: the function of the nutritive faculty is to bring to every limb from food according to its size, and to attach to it from food in equal measure. As for the growth faculty, it takes away from the body from food what is needed for increase in another direction, and attaches it to that direction so that that direction increases more than the increase of another direction. Explanation: if the nutritive faculty is isolated and its action is strong, and what it brings is more than what dissolves, then it increases the width and depth of the limbs a visible increase by fattening, and does not increase in length by a noticeable amount, while the growth faculty increases in length much more than it increases in width.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage contrasts the nutritive and growth faculties (al-ghādhiyya wa al-nāmiyya). It begins by noting that the growth faculty brings food to the limb, assimilates, and attaches it, similar to the nutritive faculty, but the nutritive faculty replaces exactly what is lost, while the growth faculty adds more. The author argues that if the nutritive faculty can obtain the original, it should also be able to obtain the additional (since the additional resembles the original), implying the nutritive faculty could perform growth. However, this is only true initially when power and moisture are abundant. The objection is raised: nutrition and growth are different actions and thus cannot stem from a single principle. In response, the author cites Ibn Sina's al-Shifā', where the nutritive faculty brings equal replacement to each limb, whereas the growth faculty redistributes food from one part to another to cause disproportionate length increase. The explanation provides an example: when the nutritive faculty acts alone and brings more than what dissolves, it increases width and depth (fattening), not length, whereas the growth faculty increases length much more than width. Thus, the two faculties are distinct.

Key Terms
النَّامِيَة
الغَاذِيَة
التَّغْذِيَة
التَّنْمِيَة
الرُّطُوبَة
المُتَحَلِّل
البَدَل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The growth faculty is simply the nutritive faculty acting with extra strength.
Correction: The passage shows that while the argument suggests this possibility initially, the objection and response later clarify that they are fundamentally different actions with distinct effects.

Checkpoint
According to the initial argument, what condition is required for the nutritive faculty to act as the growth faculty?
A) The limb must be large.
B) Power must be strong and moisture abundant.
C) The nutritive faculty must be isolated.
D) The growth faculty must be absent.

Key Takeaway
The nutritive faculty could potentially perform growth initially, but this is not the full picture.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson explains that your body's ability to grow and repair declines due to weakening power, scarce moisture, and increasing size. When was the last time you noticed this decline in yourself? How does this reality humble you in your reliance on Allah's continual creation?
?
The argument says that if you can do a thing, you can do its like. Are there good deeds you have succeeded in once but now assume you cannot repeat? What specific act of worship or character that you once mastered have you stopped doing, and what will you do today to revive it?
?
The growth faculty only excels in the beginning when conditions are ideal. Think of a spiritual 'growth' you experienced early in your Islam but now feels diminished. What change in conditions (weaker intention, less spiritual 'moisture', bigger distractions) caused the decline, and what is one concrete step you can take tonight to restore that initial strength?

6 min
Distinct Functions of Nutritive and Growth Faculties
Objection and refutation with Ibn Sina's distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that nutrition and growth are different actions and Ibn Sina's explanation of their distinct effects on limb size.

Core Idea
Nutrition increases width; growth increases length.

The previous argument is met with an objection: nutrition and growth cannot be from a single principle because they are different actions. In response, the author cites Ibn Sina's al-Shifa to clarify their distinct functions. The nutritive faculty replaces exactly what is lost in each limb, while the growth faculty redistributes food to cause disproportionate length increase. This lesson unpacks the objection and the clarifying explanation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage where it is objected that nutrition and growth are different actions and cannot stem from one principle.
The objection notes that nutrition involves replacement, similarity, and attachment, attributed to three powers.
2
Understand the response from Ibn Sina
Ibn Sina describes the nutritive faculty as bringing equal replacement to each limb, and the growth faculty as taking from one part to increase another.
3
Examine the example
The example shows that when the nutritive faculty acts alone and brings more than dissolves, it increases width and depth (fattening), not length. The growth faculty increases length much more than width.
This clearly distinguishes the two faculties.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Consider how this explanation resolves the objection by showing that the two faculties have different modes of operation and effects.
The growth faculty is not just a stronger version of nutritive; it has a different effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله وقد يقال : إشارة إلى ما ذكره الإمام من أن فعل النامية إيراد الغذاء إلى العضو، وتشبيهه به وإلصاقه كالغازية، إلا أن الغازية تفعل هذه الأفعال، بحيث يكون الوارد مساوياً للمتحلل والنامية تفعل أزيد من المتحلل. ولا شك أن القادر على الشيء، قادر على مثله ، والجزء الزائد مشابه للأصل، فإذا قويت الغاذية على تحصيل الأصل، قويت على تحصيل الزائد، وتكون هي النامية، إلا أنها في الابتداء تكون قوية على إيراد بدل الأصل والزائد معاً، لشدة القوة على الفعل، وكثرة المادة أعني الرطوبة وقلة الحاجة بواسطة صغر العضو، وبعد ذلك يعود الأمر إلى النقصان لضعف في القوة وقلة في المادة، وعظم في العضو. واعترض بأن التغذية والتنمية فعلان مختلفان فلا يستندان إلى مبدأ واحد حتى أن أمر التغذية لما كان بإيراد البدل والشبيه والالصاق أسندوه إلى قوى ثلاث، وهذا ما قال في الشفاء إن شأن الغازية أن تؤتي كل عضو من الغذاء بقدر عظمه وصغره، وتلصق به من الغذاء بمقداره الذي له على السواء. وأما النامية فتسلب جانباً من البدن من الغذاء ما يحتاج إليه لزيادة في جهة أخرى، فتلصقه بتلك الجهة لتزيد تلك الجهة فوق زيادة جهة أخرى. بيان ذلك : أن الغاذية إذا انفردت وقوي فعلها، وكان ما تورد أكثر مما يتحلل، فإنها تزيد في عرض الأعضاء وعمقها زيادة ظاهرة بالتسمين، ولا تزيد في الطول زيادة يعتد بها، والنامية تزيد في الطول أكثر كثيراً مما تزيد في العرض.
For his saying: 'And it may be said' is a reference to what the Imam mentioned, that the act of the growth faculty is to bring food to the limb, assimilate it and attach it like the nutritive faculty, except that the nutritive faculty does these actions such that what is brought equals what is dissolved, while the growth faculty does more than what is dissolved. There is no doubt that one who is capable of a thing is capable of its like, and the additional part resembles the original. So if the nutritive faculty is strong enough to obtain the original, it is strong enough to obtain the additional, and it would be the growth faculty, except that in the beginning it is strong enough to bring both the replacement of the original and the additional together, due to the intensity of power in action, abundance of matter (i.e., moisture) and little need due to the smallness of the limb, and after that the matter returns to decline due to weakness in power, scarcity of matter, and largeness of the limb. And it was objected that nutrition and growth are two different actions, so they cannot be attributed to a single principle; indeed, since the matter of nutrition involves bringing the replacement, the similar, and attachment, they attributed it to three powers. And this is what he said in al-Shifa: the function of the nutritive faculty is to bring to every limb from food according to its size, and to attach to it from food in equal measure. As for the growth faculty, it takes away from the body from food what is needed for increase in another direction, and attaches it to that direction so that that direction increases more than the increase of another direction. According to Ibn Sina, what does the growth faculty do differently from the nutritive faculty?
A) It brings more food than is dissolved, increasing width.
B) It takes food from one part of the body and attaches it to another to increase length.
C) It replaces exactly what is dissolved.

Key Takeaway
The nutritive and growth faculties are distinct: nutrition replaces equally, growth redistributes for length increase.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own spiritual and intellectual life, have you been content with mere ‘nutrition’—maintaining what you have and replacing what is lost—or are you actively ‘growing’ by redirecting your resources (time, energy, focus) to areas that need genuine increase? What is one concrete step you can take today to shift from maintenance to growth?
?
Consider a relationship or skill you are trying to develop. Are you approaching it like the nutritive faculty—adding equally across all aspects—or like the growth faculty—strategically focusing on one area for more significant increase? Which approach is needed right now, and why are you avoiding it?
?
The passage shows that the growth faculty takes from one part to give to another. What is something you need to ‘take away’ from one area of your life (e.g., comfort, distraction) in order to invest in a more important area? Be specific. Will you do it before the end of this week?

The Meaning of Natural Death and Its Necessity
Al-Taftazani defines natural death and states the necessary causal chain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani defines natural death and why it is necessary from the exhaustion of innate moisture leading to extinction of innate heat.

Core Idea
Natural death is the necessary extinction of innate heat due to the unavoidable exhaustion of innate moisture.

Al-Taftazani begins by linking the end of life to the inability to supply the body due to weakness and lack of moisture. He explains that death is defined as the cessation of the powers because the innate heat, their instrument, is extinguished. The distinction between natural and unnatural death hinges on whether the extinction is due to the exhaustion of innate moisture.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage defining death
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the definition of death as cessation of powers due to extinction of innate heat.
2
Distinguish natural from unnatural death
Notice that natural death occurs when the exhaustion of moisture causes the heat to no longer sustain the powers. Unnatural death is from other causes.
3
Understand the necessary causal chain
The passage summarizes that corruption of moisture is necessary, so extinction of heat is necessary, so cessation of powers is necessary. Reflect on why this chain is considered necessary.
This chain is the core argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ولهذا لما أدى الضعف وقلة الرطوبة إلى العجز عن إيراد البدن حل الأجل. إشارة إلى ما ذكروا في ضرورة الموت من جهة القوة الفاعلية، وفسروا الموت بتعطل القوى عن الأفعال لانطفاء الحرارة الغريزية التي هي آلتها. فإن كان ذلك لانتهاء الرطوبة الغريزية إلى حد لا يفي ما يقوم بها من الحرارة الغريزية بأمر القوى وأفعالها، فموت طبيعي، وإلا فغير طبيعي. وحاصل الكلام أن لبطلان الرطوبة الغريزية أسباباً ضرورية، فيكون ضرورياً، فيكون انطفاء الحرارة ضرورياً لبطلان مادته، فيكون تعطل القوي ضرورياً لبطلان آلتها. وتلك الأسباب مثل انتشاق الهواء المحيط للرطوبة من الخارج، ومعاونة الحرارة الغريزية من الداخل، ومعاضدة الحركات البدنية والنفسانية الضرورية في ذلك، مع عجز الطبيعة عن مقاومة تلك المتحللات بإيراد البدل دائماً، لما سبق من تناهي القوى الجسمانية، على أن هناك أمراً آخر، يعين على إطفاء الحرارة الغريزية بطريق الغمر لغلبته في الكم، وبطريق الغمر المضادته في الكيف، وهو ما يستولى من الرطوبة القريبة الباردة البلغمية بواسطة قصور الهضم. هذا ولو فرضنا فعل الغاذية أعني إيراد البدل دائماً غير متناه. فليس التحلل دائماً على حد واحد، بل يزداد يوماً فيوماً لدوام المؤثر أعني المحللات المذكورة في متأثر واحد هو الرطوبة الغريزية، فالبدل لا يقاومه. فبالضرورة يتأدى الأمر إلى افناء التحلل للرطوبة. بل لو فرضنا البدل دائماً على مقدار المتحلل فلا اخفاء في أنه لا يقاومه لقصوره بحسب الكيفية لأن الرطوبة الغريزية تخمدت ونضجت في أوعية
He said: And for this reason, when weakness and lack of moisture lead to incapacity to supply the body, the term ends. A reference to what they mentioned about the necessity of death from the perspective of the active power, and they interpreted death as the cessation of the powers from their actions due to the extinction of the innate heat which is their instrument. If that is due to the exhaustion of the innate moisture to a degree that the innate heat which subsists by it cannot fulfill the matter of the powers and their actions, then it is a natural death; otherwise, it is unnatural. The gist of the discussion is that the corruption of the innate moisture has necessary causes, so it is necessary, so the extinction of heat is necessary due to the corruption of its matter, so the cessation of the powers is necessary due to the corruption of their instrument. And those causes are like the inhaling of the surrounding air for moisture from outside, the assistance of the innate heat from inside, and the aiding of the necessary bodily and psychic movements in that, along with the inability of nature to resist those dissolutions by supplying replacement continuously, due to the previously mentioned finitude of physical powers, and moreover there is another matter that helps in extinguishing the innate heat by way of overwhelming due to its predominance in quantity, and by way of overwhelming due to its opposition in quality, and that is the domination of the near, cold, phlegmatic moisture due to the deficiency of digestion. This, and even if we suppose the action of the nutritive faculty, i.e., supplying replacement continuously without limit, still dissolution is not always at a constant rate, but increases day by day due to the continuity of the effect, i.e., the mentioned dissolvents, upon a single affected thing, which is the innate moisture, so the replacement does not resist it. So necessarily the matter leads to the annihilation of the moisture by dissolution. Moreover, even if we suppose the replacement always equals the dissolved, it is clear that it does not resist it due to its deficiency in quality, because the innate moisture becomes extinguished and ripened in the vessels.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage discusses the natural necessity of death. It begins by stating that when weakness and lack of moisture prevent the body from being supplied, the term of life ends. This refers to the active power (al-quwwa al-fāʿiliyya): death is the cessation of the powers due to the extinction of innate heat (al-ḥarāra al-gharīziyya), which is their instrument. If this extinction results from the exhaustion of innate moisture (al-ruṭūba al-gharīziyya) to a point where the heat subsisting by it can no longer sustain the powers, it is natural death; otherwise, it is unnatural. The core point: the corruption of innate moisture has necessary causes, so it is necessary; consequently, the extinction of heat is necessary (due to corruption of its matter), and the cessation of powers is necessary (due to corruption of their instrument). The causes include: external air absorbing moisture, internal heat assisting, and necessary bodily and psychic movements, all while nature cannot continuously resist these dissolutions because physical powers are finite. Additionally, cold phlegmatic moisture from poor digestion overwhelms and extinguishes the heat quantitatively and qualitatively. Even if the nutritive faculty supplied replacement endlessly, dissolution increases daily due to continuous dissolvents acting on the single subject (innate moisture), so replacement fails to resist. Even if replacement equaled dissolution, it would still fail due to qualitative deficiency, as innate moisture becomes extinguished and ripened in the vessels.

Key Terms
المَوْت
الحَرَارَة الغَرِيزِيَّة
الرُّطُوبَة الغَرِيزِيَّة
القُوَّة الفَاعِلِيَّة
المَوْت الطَّبِيعِيّ
الانْطِفَاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Death is always caused by external accidents.
Correction: This passage argues that natural death results from the necessary internal exhaustion of innate moisture, not merely external events.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes natural death from unnatural death?
A) Natural death occurs when the innate heat is extinguished due to exhaustion of innate moisture; unnatural death occurs from other causes.
B) Natural death happens in sleep; unnatural death happens when awake.
C) Natural death is painless; unnatural death is painful.
D) Natural death is caused by external air; unnatural death is caused by internal heat.

Key Takeaway
Natural death is a necessary consequence of the finite nature of innate moisture, which supports the innate heat that powers all bodily functions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If natural death is a necessary consequence of the exhaustion of your body's innate moisture, are you living in a way that acknowledges the finite nature of your physical existence? What specific change can you make today to prepare for the inevitable end?
?
The passage states that death is the cessation of your powers because their instrument (innate heat) is extinguished. Reflect on your own powers—your intellect, strength, health. Are you using them in a way that pleases Allah, knowing that they will cease? What can you do to ensure that your actions are not merely for this world but for the Hereafter?
?
Consider the logical chain: moisture exhaustion leads to heat extinction leads to power cessation. This is inescapable. Does this realization cause you to fear death, or does it motivate you to live with purpose? How can you transform your awareness of death into a productive urgency in worship and good deeds?
