Definition of the Sense of Touch and Its Properties
The author defines the sense of touch and its properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical definition of the sense of touch, its properties as a pervasive power, and its purpose of distinguishing harm from benefit.

Core Idea
Touch is a vital sense that pervades the body and distinguishes beneficial from harmful contacts.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid presents the classical philosophical definition of the sense of touch. The author explains its nature as a power pervading the body, its necessity for survival, and its exclusive operation through direct contact. This definition helps us appreciate the teleological design of the senses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise, technical style typical of philosophical texts.
2
Identify the key properties of touch
The passage describes what touch perceives (heat, cold, etc.) and how it functions (through nerves, pervading most of the body except certain organs). List these properties.
The author also provides an analogy to the nutritive power in plants.
3
Reflect on the purpose of touch
The author argues that touch must operate by contact only, otherwise it could not distinguish harmful from beneficial. Ask yourself why this distinction is crucial for survival.
This argument highlights the wisdom (hikma) in the design of the sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (أما الحواس الظاهرة فمنها اللمس : وهي قوة سارية في البدن تدرك بها الحرارة والبرودة، ونحوهما عند المماسة وهي للحيوان في محل الضرورة، كالغاذية للنبات، ولذا كانت بمعونة العصب سارية في جميع الأعضاء، سوى ما يتضرر به كالكبد والطحال، والكلية والرئة والعظم، وكان الحيوان يبقى عند بطلان سائر الحواس دونها . هي قوة تأتي في الأعصاب إلى جميع الجلد، وأكثر اللحم والغشاء، من شأنها إدراك الحرارة والبرودة، والرطوبة واليبوسة، والخشونة والملاسة، ونحو ذلك، بأن ينفعل عنها العضو اللامس عند المماسة بحكم الاستقراء، ولأنها لو أدركت البعيد أيضاً، لم يحصل التمييز بين ما يجب دفعه، وما لا يجب فيفوت الغرض من خلق اللامسة. أعني رفع الضار، وجلب النافع .
He said: (As for the external senses, among them is touch:) It is a power pervading the body by which one perceives heat and cold and their like upon contact. It is for the animal in a place of necessity, like the nutritive power for plants. Therefore it was with the help of nerves, pervading all the limbs, except those that would be harmed by it, like the liver, spleen, kidney, lung, and bone. And the animal remains when other senses are lost but not without it. It is a power that comes through the nerves to all the skin, most of the flesh, and the membrane, whose function is to perceive heat, cold, moisture, dryness, roughness, smoothness, and the like, by the touching organ being affected by them upon contact, by way of induction. And because if it perceived the distant as well, no distinction would be made between what must be repelled and what need not, and thus the purpose of creating touch would be lost—i.e., repelling harm and attracting benefit.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage defines the sense of touch (al-lams) as one of the external senses. The author describes it as a power (quwwa) that pervades the entire body, perceiving tangible qualities like heat, cold, etc. upon contact (mumassa). He states its necessity for animals is analogous to the nutritive power for plants. It is assisted by nerves and pervades all limbs except those that would be harmed by it (like liver, spleen, kidney, lung, bone). The animal can survive without other senses but not without touch. Then he elaborates that touch perceives qualities such as heat, cold, moisture, dryness, roughness, smoothness, etc. by the affected organ. If touch could also perceive distant objects, it would fail to distinguish between what must be repelled and what need not, defeating its purpose of repelling harm and attracting benefit.

الحواس الظاهرة

Correction: Touch pervades the entire body except certain internal organs like the liver, spleen, kidney, lung, and bone, which would be harmed by it.

Why Touch is the Essential Sense for Animals
Ibn Sina argues that touch is essential for animal life and explains its necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's argument that touch is the fundamental sense necessary for animal life and why it is absent in certain internal organs.

Core Idea
Touch is the scout that alerts the soul to what corrupts the body's temperament.

This lesson presents Ibn Sina's argument for the primacy of touch. He claims that touch is not merely one sense among equals, but the very sense that makes an animal an animal. The passage explains why touch is indispensable, how it relates to the animal's temperament, and why it is absent in certain organs like the liver, spleen, kidney, and lung.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the full argument sink in before analyzing.
The passage is a direct quote from Ibn Sina.
2
Identify the main claim and analogy
Find where Ibn Sina states that touch is the first sense that makes an animal an animal, and the analogy to plants' nutritive power.
The analogy establishes necessity.
3
Understand the reasoning
Examine why touch is the first scout: because the animal's temperament is made of tangible qualities, and corruption comes from their variation.
Sensation acts as a scout for the soul.
4
Note the exceptions
Identify which organs lack touch and why – liver, spleen, kidney, and lung – and the reasons given.
Lack of sensation is beneficial in these cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واللامسة للحيوان فى محل الضرورة، كالغاذية للنبات . قال ابن سينا : أول الحواس الذي يصير به الحيوان حيواناً هو اللمس ، فإنه كما أنه للنبات قوة غاذية ، يجوز أن تفقد سائر القوى دونها، كذلك حال اللامسة للحيوان لأن مزاجه من الكيفيات الملموسة، وفساده باختلافها، والحس طليعة للنفس، فيجب أن تكون الطليعة الأولى هو ما يدل على ما يقع به الفساد، ويحفظ به الصلاح ، وأن يكون قبل الطلائع التي تدل على أمور تتعلق ببعضها منفعة خارجة عند القوام، أو مضرة خارجة عن الفساد والذوق ، وإن كان دالاً على الشيء الذي به تستبقى الحياة من المطعومات، فقد يجوز أن يبقى الحيوان بدونه، بإرشاد الحواس الآخر على الغذاء الموافق، واجتناب المضاد وليس شيء منها يعين على أن الهواء المحيط بالبدن ،محرق أو مجمد. وبالجملة فالجوع شهوة الحار اليابس والعطش شهوة البارد الرطب، والغذاء ما يتكيف بهذه الكيفيات اللمسية، وأما الطعوم فتطيبات . فلذلك كثيراً ما يبطل حس الذوق أو غيره، ويبقى الحيوان حيواناً بخلاف اللمس، ولشدة الاحتياج إليه، كان بمعونة الأعصاب، سارياً في جميع الأعضاء، إلا ما يكون عدم الحس أنفع له كالكبد والطحال والكلية ، لئلا يتأذى بما يلاقيها من الحار اللاذع . فإن الكبد مولد للصفراء، والسوداء، والطحال والكلية معينان لما فيه لذع، وكالرئة فإنها دائمة الحركة، فتتألم باصطكاك بعضها ببعض، ومولد للأبخرة الحادة،
And touch is for the animal in a place of necessity, like the nutritive power for plants. Ibn Sina said: The first sense by which an animal becomes an animal is touch. For just as plants have a nutritive power, and it is possible that all other powers be lost without it, so is the case of touch for the animal. Because its temperament is from the tangible qualities, and its corruption is by their variation. And sensation is a scout for the soul, so the first scout must be that which indicates what causes corruption and preserves soundness, and it should be before the scouts that indicate matters concerning which some external benefit or harm is incidental to the constitution, or external harm apart from corruption, and taste, although it indicates the thing by which life is sustained from foodstuffs, it is possible for the animal to survive without it, by the guidance of the other senses to suitable food and avoidance of what is harmful. And there is none of them that informs about whether the surrounding air is burning or freezing. In summary, hunger is the desire for hot and dry, thirst is the desire for cold and moist, and food is what is qualified by these tangible qualities. As for tastes, they are flavorings. Therefore, often the sense of taste or other senses are lost, and the animal remains an animal, unlike touch. And because of the intense need for it, it was with the help of nerves, pervading all the limbs, except those for which lack of sensation is more beneficial, like the liver, spleen, and kidney, lest they be hurt by the hot pungent [substances] that meet them. For the liver generates bile and black bile, and the spleen and kidney assist in what has pungency, and like the lung, for it is constantly moving and would suffer pain from its parts striking each other, and it generates sharp vapors.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of touch, quoting Ibn Sina to emphasize its fundamental role. The author repeats that touch is necessary for animals like nutritive power for plants. Ibn Sina argues that the first sense constituting animality is touch, because an animal's temperament (mizaj) is formed from tangible qualities (kayfiyyat mulumasa) and corruption (fasad) occurs through their variation. Sensation serves as a scout (tali'a) for the soul, and the primary scout must indicate what causes corruption and preserves health, preceding scouts for external benefits or harms. Taste, though it indicates food, is not essential; animals can survive without it by using other senses to find suitable food. However, no other sense informs about dangerous air temperatures. Hunger and thirst are desires for specific tangible qualities, and tastes are secondary flavorings. Thus, taste can be lost while the animal remains alive, but not touch. Due to extreme need, touch pervades all limbs via nerves, except where lack of sensation is beneficial: liver, spleen, kidney (to avoid pain from pungent substances they handle), and lung (to avoid pain from constant movement and sharp vapors).

Key Terms
ابن سينا
طليعة
مزاج
الكيفيات الملموسة
الأبخرة الحادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Touch is just one sense among equals; other senses are equally essential for animal life.
Correction: Ibn Sina argues that touch is the most essential sense for animality because it directly protects the temperament from corruption, and animals can survive without other senses but not without touch.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina, why is touch the first sense that makes an animal an animal?
A) Because it is the most pleasurable
B) Because the animal's temperament is from tangible qualities and corruption by their variation
C) Because it is the most powerful
D) Because it is located in the skin

Key Takeaway
Touch is the primary sense because it directly guards the animal's temperament from corruption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body’s very life depends on the sense of touch. When was the last time you thanked Allah for the ability to feel heat and cold, which alerts you to danger? How would your life change if you lost that awareness? Reflect on whether you neglect this gift.
?
Ibn Sina calls sensation a scout for the soul. Are you paying attention to the messages your body sends you—like discomfort, pain, or fatigue—or are you ignoring them? What might be the spiritual consequences of neglecting that scout, not just physically but in your relationship with your Creator?
?
The passage notes that touch is absent in certain organs to prevent unnecessary pain. This reveals a wise design in your body. Do you see this as a sign of a purposeful Creator? How should this knowledge affect your trust in Allah’s wisdom and your gratitude for the way He has made you?

Touch: The First and Most Essential Sense for Animals
Explains why touch is the first sense and necessary for survival

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the sense of touch is considered the first and most essential sense for animal survival according to the kalam tradition.

Core Idea
Touch is the animal's primary vanguard, indispensable for life.

The human body is equipped with multiple senses, but which one is most fundamental? This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid argues that touch is not only the first sense to emerge but also the one without which an animal cannot survive. Drawing on Ibn Sina's authority, the author explains why touch is the vanguard of the soul, essential for detecting harm and preserving life.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening claim
The passage begins by stating the purpose of touch: to repel harm and attract benefit. Without touch, this purpose is defeated.
2
Compare touch to plant nutrition
Notice the analogy: touch is to animals as the nutritive faculty is to plants. Just as a plant can lose all other powers but not nutrition, an animal can lose other senses but not touch.
This analogy reinforces the necessity of touch.
3
Understand Ibn Sina's definition
Ibn Sina is quoted to define touch as the first sense that makes an animal an animal. Reflect on why he says 'first' and what makes touch unique.
Ibn Sina's authority is invoked to support the argument.
4
Analyze the vanguard argument
The passage explains that sensation is the vanguard of the soul, so the first vanguard must indicate corruption and preservation. This logical argument places touch before other senses.
This is a key logical step.
5
Distinguish touch from taste
The author argues that taste is merely a seasoning, while touch deals with fundamental qualities like heat and moisture. Taste can be lost without death, but touch cannot.
This distinction solidifies touch's primacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البعيد أيضاً، لم يحصل التمييز بين ما يجب دفعه، وما لا يجب فيفوت الغرض من خلق اللامسة. أعني رفع الضار، وجلب النافع. واللامسة للحيوان في محل الضرورة، كالغاذية للنبات. قال ابن سينا: أول الحواس الذي يصير به الحيوان حيواناً هو اللمس، فإنه كما أنه للنبات قوة غاذية، يجوز أن تفقد سائر القوى دونها، كذلك حال اللامسة للحيوان لأن مزاجه من الكيفيات الملموسة، وفساده باختلافها، والحس طليعة للنفس، فيجب أن تكون الطليعة الأولى هو ما يدل على ما يقع به الفساد، ويحفظ به الصلاح، وأن يكون قبل الطلائع التي تدل على أمور تتعلق ببعضها منفعة خارجة عند القوام، أو مضرة خارجة عن الفساد والذوق، وإن كان دالاً على الشيء الذي به تستبقى الحياة من المطعومات، فقد يجوز أن يبقى الحيوان بدونه، بإرشاد الحواس الأخرى على الغذاء الموافق، واجتناب المضاد وليس شيء منها يعين على أن الهواء المحيط بالبدن محرق أو مجمد. وبالجملة فالجوع شهوة الحار اليابس والعطش شهوة البارد الرطب، والغذاء ما يتكيف بهذه الكيفيات اللمسية، وأما الطعوم فتطيبات. فلذلك كثيراً ما يبطل حس الذوق أو غيره، ويبقى الحيوان حيواناً بخلاف اللمس، ولشدة الاحتياج إليه، كان بمعونة الأعصاب، سارياً في جميع الأعضاء، إلا ما يكون عدم الحس أنفع له كالكبد والطحال والكلية، لئلا يتأذى بما يلاقيها من الحار اللاذع. فإن الكبد مولد للصفراء والسوداء، والطحال والكلية معينان لما فيه لذع، وكالرئة فإنها دائمة الحركة، فتتألم باصطكاك بعضها ببعض، ومولد للأبخرة الحادة، ومصعد للمواد، فيتأذى بذلك، وكالعظام فإنها أساس البدن، ودعامة الحركات، بمعنى أنها تجعل الحركات أشد، بجعل أعضائها أقوى، فلو أحست لتألمت بالضغط والمزاحمة، وبما يرد عليه من المصاكات.
Also the distant [objects] are not distinguished between what must be repelled and what not, so the purpose of creating touch is missed, I mean repelling the harmful and attracting the beneficial. And touch is for animals in the place of necessity, like the nutritive faculty for plants. Ibn Sina said: The first of the senses by which the animal becomes an animal is touch, for just as for plants there is a nutritive power which can lose all other powers while it remains, so is the case of touch for animals, because its temperament is from the tangible qualities, and its corruption is through their variation, and sensation is the vanguard of the soul, so it is necessary that the first vanguard be that which indicates what causes corruption and preserves soundness, and that it be before the vanguards that indicate matters related to some external benefit for the constitution, or harm external to corruption and taste. And although it indicates the thing by which life is preserved from foods, it may be that the animal can survive without it, by the guidance of the other senses to suitable food and avoidance of the opposite. But none of them indicates whether the air surrounding the body is burning or freezing. In summary: hunger is the appetite for the hot and dry, thirst is the appetite for the cold and moist, and food is what is conditioned by these tangible qualities. As for tastes, they are seasonings. Therefore often the sense of taste or other is lost while the animal remains an animal, unlike touch. And due to the intensity of need for it, it is with the assistance of nerves, running through all limbs, except where the lack of sensation is more beneficial, like the liver, spleen, and kidneys, lest they be harmed by the hot, stinging [substances] that meet them. For the liver generates bile and black bile, and the spleen and kidneys help with what has sting. And like the lung, for it is in constant motion, so it would suffer pain from the friction of its parts against each other, and it generates sharp vapors, and it is an outlet and an elevator for materials, so it would be harmed by that. And like the bones, for they are the foundation of the body and the support of movements, meaning they make movements stronger by making their organs stronger. So if they were sentient, they would be pained by pressure and crowding and by the jolts that come upon them.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage explains why the sense of touch is the first and most essential sense for animal survival. The author begins by stating that without touch, the animal cannot distinguish between harmful and beneficial contacts, thus defeating the purpose of its creation. He then compares touch in animals to the nutritive faculty in plants: just as a plant can lose all other powers while retaining nutrition, an animal can lose other senses but still live, but cannot live without touch. Ibn Sina is quoted to support this: touch makes an animal an animal, because its temperament consists of tangible qualities and corruption occurs through them. Sensation is the vanguard of the soul, so the first vanguard must indicate what causes corruption or preserves soundness. Other senses indicate external benefits or harms, but touch is fundamental. For example, taste can be lost and the animal still survives by guidance from other senses, but none of them can determine if the surrounding air is burning or freezing. Then the author explains that hunger and thirst are appetites for hot-dry and cold-moist qualities, respectively, and food is conditioned by these tactile qualities—tastes are mere seasonings. Thus, animals often lose taste or other senses but remain animals, unlike touch. Because of the extreme need for touch, it pervades all body parts via nerves, except where lack of sensation is more beneficial, such as the liver, spleen, kidneys, lungs, and bones. Each of these organs would be harmed by constant painful stimuli (e.g., sharp substances, friction, pressure) if they were sentient, so their insensibility is a mercy.

Key Terms
اللامسة
القوة الغاذية
الحس
اللمس
الكيفيات الملموسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that taste or sight is more important for survival. However, the author shows that touch is more fundamental because it deals with basic life-sustaining qualities like temperature, while taste is only for seasoning.
Correction: Taste, like the other senses, is secondary; touch is primary because it directly preserves the animal's temperament.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is touch considered the first sense that makes an animal an animal?
A) Because it is located in the skin and covers the whole body.
B) Because without touch, the animal cannot distinguish harmful from beneficial contacts, and its temperament is directly affected by tangible qualities.
C) Because it is the only sense that works automatically without conscious effort.
D) Because it is the sense that allows animals to taste food.

Key Takeaway
Touch is the foundational sense without which animal life cannot be sustained; it detects the tangible qualities essential for survival.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the sense of taste is merely a 'seasoning' and not essential for survival, what does that reveal about the priorities in your life? Are you pursuing spiritual 'seasonings' while neglecting the essential 'touch' of your soul—the immediate awareness of what harms or benefits you?
?
The passage says touch is the first vanguard that indicates 'what causes corruption and preserves soundness.' In your daily life, what corrupting influences are you failing to sense because you have dulled this inner 'touch'? How can you sharpen it starting today?
?
Imagine losing the sense of touch. Your entire world—including the ability to tell if the air is burning or freezing—would be lost. Yet you have never once thanked Allah for it. What would it take for you to truly appreciate this gift, and what accountability does that create for how you use your body?

Why Some Internal Organs Lack Sensation
Explains the exceptions to the rule of touch pervading the body

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reasoning behind why certain internal organs (liver, spleen, kidneys, lungs, bones) lack the sense of touch despite its pervasiveness in the body.

Core Idea
Lack of sensation in some organs is a mercy, preventing pain from vital functions.

The previous lesson established that touch pervades the entire body via nerves. But are there exceptions? This passage explains that some internal organs are deliberately without sensation to avoid constant pain from their harsh environments. The author provides a detailed account of why the liver, spleen, kidneys, lungs, and bones are insensate, demonstrating the wisdom in their design.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening explanation
The passage begins by stating that touch runs through all limbs via nerves, but there are exceptions where lack of sensation is more beneficial.
2
Identify the organs without sensation
The author lists the liver, spleen, kidneys, lungs, and bones. Notice the reasoning for each.
These organs are involved in processes with potentially painful stimuli.
3
Understand the reason for each
For each organ, the author explains what would cause pain if it were sentient: the liver generates bile, the spleen/kidneys handle stinging substances, the lung moves and produces vapors, bones endure pressure.
Think about why each function would be harmful if felt.
4
Reflect on the wisdom
Consider the mercy in this design: if these organs were sentient, the animal would be in constant pain, hindering survival.
This shows the intricate design of the body.

Source Text

Refuting the Attribution of Touch to Celestial Bodies
Refutes the claim that celestial bodies have touch.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why celestial bodies cannot be attributed with the sense of touch based on the argument that life necessitates touch.

Core Idea
Life does not automatically imply touch, especially for celestial bodies.

Some scholars argued that since celestial bodies have life (due to their psychic motions), they must possess touch. This lesson examines that argument and its refutation, showing that touch is not a necessary concomitant of life.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: claim, supporting argument, objection, reply, and final answer.
The passage is part of a larger refutation.
2
Identify the claim and argument
The claim: celestial bodies have touch. The argument: touch is a necessary concomitant of life; celestial bodies have life because their motions are psychic; therefore they must have perception and touch.
Note the key phrase: 'في كون حركاتها نفسانية'.
3
Understand the objection and reply
Opponents object that touch serves to attract agreeable and repel contrary, which is pointless in the celestial sphere (no generation/corruption). The reply: in celestial bodies, touch could exist for other purposes like enjoyment of touching and friction.
This shows that the function of touch is not limited to earthly needs.
4
Grasp the final answer
The definitive refutation: touch is not a necessary concomitant of life absolutely. Life does not necessarily entail touch; so the inference from life to touch is invalid.
This is the key kalam principle: not all concomitants are necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وأثبتها بعضهم للفلكيات وبعضهم للبسائط العنصرية. أي القوة اللامسة بعضهم للفلكيات زعماً منهم أنها من توابع الحياة، وللأفلاك حياة، لكون حركاتها نفسانية، فيكون لها شعور ولمس بالضرورة. والقول بأنها إنما تكون بجذب الملائم، ودفع المنافي، فيكون وجودها في الفلك الممتنع عليه الكون والفساد معطلاً مردود بأن ذلك إنما هو في الأرضيات، وأما في الفلكيات فيجوز أن توجد لغرض آخر كتلذذها بالملامسة والاصطكاك. والجواب: منع كونها من لوازم الحياة على الإطلاق. وأما ما ذهب إليه البعض من الملامسة للعنصريات بناء على أن الأرض تهوى من العلو إلى السفل على نهج واحد، والنار بالعكس، وذلك يدل على شعورهما بالملائم وغير الملائم ففي غاية الضعف.
He said: And some affirmed it for the celestial bodies and some for the simple elemental [bodies]. That is, the power of touch: some affirmed it for celestial bodies, claiming that it is among the concomitants of life, and the celestial spheres have life because their motions are psychic, so they necessarily have perception and touch. And the statement that it [touch] only exists for attracting the agreeable and repelling the contrary, so its existence in the celestial sphere, upon which generation and corruption are impossible, would be futile, is refuted by [the reply] that this is only in earthly things; as for celestial things, it may exist for another purpose, such as their enjoyment of touching and friction. And the answer: the denial that it is among the necessary concomitants of life absolutely. As for what some have held of touch for elemental bodies, based on the fact that the earth falls from above to below in one manner, and fire conversely, and that indicates their perception of the agreeable and disagreeable, it is extremely weak.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the claim that celestial bodies (al-falakiyāt) and simple elemental bodies (al-basā'iṭ al-ʿunṣuriyya) possess the sense of touch. The author first states that some have affirmed touch for celestial bodies, arguing that touch is a necessary concomitant of life and that celestial spheres are alive because their motions are psychic (nafsāniyya), so they must have perception and touch. They further argue that the function of touch (attracting the agreeable and repelling the contrary) would be pointless in the celestial sphere because it is immune to generation and corruption. The author refutes this by pointing out that this function is specific to earthly bodies; in celestial bodies, touch could exist for another purpose, such as delight in touching and friction. Then the author gives the definitive answer: the denial that touch is a necessary concomitant of life absolutely. This means one cannot automatically infer touch from life, especially for celestial beings. Next, the author mentions the weaker claim that simple elemental bodies (like earth and fire) have touch, based on their natural motions (earth falls straight down, fire rises straight up), which supposedly indicates they perceive what is agreeable (like their natural place) and disagreeable. The author dismisses this as extremely weak. The passage thus upholds the view that touch is confined to animals on earth and not extended to other types of bodies.

Key Terms
الفلكيات
لازم الحياة
الشعور
الملامسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any living being must have the sense of touch because it is a necessary concomitant of life.
Correction: Life does not necessarily entail touch; the author denies that touch is a necessary concomitant of life absolutely, so the inference from life to touch is invalid.

Checkpoint
What is the final answer given by the author to refute the claim that celestial bodies have touch?
A) Touch is not a necessary concomitant of life absolutely.
B) Celestial bodies do not have psychic motions.
C) Touch would be pointless in the celestial sphere.
D) Celestial bodies experience pleasure from friction.

Key Takeaway
Life does not necessarily require touch; the claim that celestial bodies have touch is refuted by denying that touch is a necessary concomitant of life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that because something is alive it must feel pain or pleasure in the same way you do? How does this lesson challenge your understanding of life in creatures different from you? Will you now be more careful in making analogies about Allah's creation?
?
The author argues that life does not necessarily require touch. In your own life, do you sometimes attribute unnecessary conditions to your faith or actions? What 'necessary concomitant' might you be wrongly assuming about your relationship with Allah?
?
This lesson refutes an inference based on incomplete reasoning. Consider a belief you hold about Allah or His creation that you may have adopted without rigorous proof. Are you willing to subject that belief to the same scrutiny as the scholars did here?

Refuting the Attribution of Touch to Elemental Bodies
Refutes the claim that simple elemental bodies have touch.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribution of touch to simple elemental bodies is considered extremely weak.

Core Idea
Natural motion alone does not prove perception.

A different group claimed that earth and fire exhibit touch because their natural motions indicate awareness of agreeable and disagreeable places. This lesson shows why that reasoning is flawed and deemed extremely weak.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and translation. Notice the concise dismissal of the argument.
This is a short but important refutation.
2
Understand the claim and evidence
The claim: elemental bodies like earth and fire have touch. The evidence: earth falls straight down, fire rises straight up, showing they perceive what is agreeable (their natural place) and disagreeable (opposite).
The evidence is based on regular motion patterns.
3
Grasp the refutation
The author dismisses this argument as extremely weak. The reasoning is flawed because regular motion does not necessitate perception; it could be due to innate dispositions without awareness.
This shows the limits of analogical reasoning from motion to perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وأثبتها بعضهم للفلكيات وبعضهم للبسائط العنصرية. أي القوة اللامسة بعضهم للفلكيات زعماً منهم أنها من توابع الحياة، وللأفلاك حياة، لكون حركاتها نفسانية، فيكون لها شعور ولمس بالضرورة. والقول بأنها إنما تكون بجذب الملائم، ودفع المنافي، فيكون وجودها في الفلك الممتنع عليه الكون والفساد معطلاً مردود بأن ذلك إنما هو في الأرضيات، وأما في الفلكيات فيجوز أن توجد لغرض آخر كتلذذها بالملامسة والاصطكاك. والجواب: منع كونها من لوازم الحياة على الإطلاق. وأما ما ذهب إليه البعض من الملامسة للعنصريات بناء على أن الأرض تهوى من العلو إلى السفل على نهج واحد، والنار بالعكس، وذلك يدل على شعورهما بالملائم وغير الملائم ففي غاية الضعف.
He said: And some affirmed it for the celestial bodies and some for the simple elemental [bodies]. That is, the power of touch: some affirmed it for celestial bodies, claiming that it is among the concomitants of life, and the celestial spheres have life because their motions are psychic, so they necessarily have perception and touch. And the statement that it [touch] only exists for attracting the agreeable and repelling the contrary, so its existence in the celestial sphere, upon which generation and corruption are impossible, would be futile, is refuted by [the reply] that this is only in earthly things; as for celestial things, it may exist for another purpose, such as their enjoyment of touching and friction. And the answer: the denial that it is among the necessary concomitants of life absolutely. As for what some have held of touch for elemental bodies, based on the fact that the earth falls from above to below in one manner, and fire conversely, and that indicates their perception of the agreeable and disagreeable, it is extremely weak.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the claim that celestial bodies (al-falakiyāt) and simple elemental bodies (al-basā'iṭ al-ʿunṣuriyya) possess the sense of touch. The author first states that some have affirmed touch for celestial bodies, arguing that touch is a necessary concomitant of life and that celestial spheres are alive because their motions are psychic (nafsāniyya), so they must have perception and touch. They further argue that the function of touch (attracting the agreeable and repelling the contrary) would be pointless in the celestial sphere because it is immune to generation and corruption. The author refutes this by pointing out that this function is specific to earthly bodies; in celestial bodies, touch could exist for another purpose, such as delight in touching and friction. Then the author gives the definitive answer: the denial that touch is a necessary concomitant of life absolutely. This means one cannot automatically infer touch from life, especially for celestial beings. Next, the author mentions the weaker claim that simple elemental bodies (like earth and fire) have touch, based on their natural motions (earth falls straight down, fire rises straight up), which supposedly indicates they perceive what is agreeable (like their natural place) and disagreeable. The author dismisses this as extremely weak. The passage thus upholds the view that touch is confined to animals on earth and not extended to other types of bodies.

غير الملائم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The predictable motion of elements like earth falling down and fire rising up proves that they have perception of agreeable and disagreeable, and therefore touch.
Correction: Regular motion does not require perception; it could be due to natural inclination without awareness. The author considers this argument extremely weak.

Checkpoint
On what basis did some scholars claim that elemental bodies have touch?
A) The regular motion of earth and fire shows they perceive what is agreeable and disagreeable.
B) Elemental bodies have organs for touch.
C) They feel pain when moved from their natural place.
D) They have psychic motions like celestial bodies.

Key Takeaway
Regular natural motion is insufficient to prove perception or the sense of touch in elemental bodies; such reasoning is considered extremely weak.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone argues that because a habit or pattern is consistent it must be deliberate, how will you respond after this lesson? Have you ever attributed intentionality to a lifeless object or a mindless routine in your own life? What evidence would you now demand before assuming consciousness behind motion?
?
This lesson shows that uniform behavior does not prove perception. How does this distinction affect your understanding of the difference between animate beings and inanimate matter? In what areas of your life do you need to stop assigning feelings or motives to things that are simply following their nature?
?
Consider your own spiritual journey: Are there acts of worship you perform out of mere habit (like the natural motion of elements) rather than conscious awareness? How can this lesson remind you to verify that your obedience is driven by genuine perception of what pleases Allah, not just mindless routine?

Ibn Sina's View on the Multiplicity of the Sense of Touch
Presents Ibn Sina's argument for multiple touch senses and the majority's opposing view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's argument for multiple touch senses and the majority's counterargument.

Core Idea
Diversity in contrariety may imply multiple senses, but most scholars insist on a single touch faculty.

Ibn Sina proposed an intriguing idea: the sense of touch might actually be multiple senses, because the types of contrariety among tangible qualities differ. The majority of scholars, however, held that touch is a single power. This lesson examines both positions and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: first Ibn Sina's view, then the majority opinion, then a note from the Canon.
The passage is incomplete; the final sentence cuts off.
2
Identify the two main positions
Clearly distinguish Ibn Sina's inclination toward multiplicity and the majority's insistence on unity. What is the key difference?
3
Analyze Ibn Sina's argument
Ibn Sina uses the example of different types of contrariety (heat/cold vs. moisture/dryness) to argue for multiple senses. How does this differ from tastes?
Contrariety is a key concept here.
4
Understand the majority's counterargument
The majority argues that difference in perceived objects does not necessitate difference in perception. Why might they say this?
Compare with other senses like sight.
5
Note the incomplete ending
The passage mentions Ibn Sina's Canon but cuts off. What might the complete sentence say about the majority's view?
This is speculation but helps engage with the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ومال ابن سينا إلى تعددها حسب تعدد التضاد بين الملموسات، فإن بين الحرارة والبرودة نوعاً من التضاد، غير الذي بين الرطوبة واليبوسة مثلاً، بخلاف تضاد الطعوم. الجمهور على أن اللامسة قوة واحدة بها تدرك جميع الملموسات، كسائر الحواس، فإن اختلاف المدركات لا يوجب اختلاف الإدراكات، ليستدل بذلك على تعدد مبانيها، وذكر ابن سينا في القانون أن أكثر المحصلين على أن
He said: And Ibn Sina inclined toward its multiplicity according to the multiplicity of contrariety among tangible things, for between heat and cold there is a type of contrariety different from that between moisture and dryness, for example, unlike the contrariety of tastes. The majority are of the opinion that the touch is a single power by which all tangible things are perceived, like the other senses, for difference of perceived objects does not necessitate difference of perceptions—lest one infer thereby the multiplicity of their [i.e., the sense organs'] structures. And Ibn Sina mentioned in the Canon that most investigators [hold that]
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents the disagreement over whether the sense of touch is a single faculty or multiple faculties. Ibn Sina inclined toward multiplicity, arguing that there are different types of contrariety (taḍādd) among tangible qualities. For example, the contrariety between heat and cold is different in kind from that between moisture and dryness. This is unlike tastes, where the contrariety is of a single type. Therefore, Ibn Sina suggested that touch might be multiple senses corresponding to these different contrarieties. The majority (al-jumhūr) hold that touch is a single power (quwwa wāḥida) that perceives all tangible objects, just like the other senses (sight, hearing, etc.). Their reasoning is that a difference in perceived objects (mudrakāt) does not necessitate a difference in the act of perception (idrākāt) itself; if it did, one could argue for multiple sense structures (mabānī). The passage then notes that Ibn Sina himself, in his famous medical work the Canon, mentioned that most investigators (akthar al-muḥaṣṣilīn) actually hold the view that touch is a single sense, which suggests even Ibn Sina's own view may be a minority position. However, the passage cuts off before completing the sentence.

Key Terms
التضاد
الملموسات
الإدراكات
تعدد المباني
اللامسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that because tangible qualities are diverse, the sense must be multiple.
Correction: The majority argues that diversity of perceived objects does not require diversity of the faculty itself.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for Ibn Sina's inclination toward the multiplicity of the sense of touch?
A) The sense of touch is located in different organs and therefore must be multiple.
B) The contrariety among tangible qualities is of different types, unlike the single type of contrariety in tastes.
C) The majority of scholars held that touch is multiple.
D) Touch can perceive both hot and cold simultaneously.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina argued for multiple touch senses based on diverse types of contrariety, but the majority held that a single power perceives all tangible qualities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows Ibn Sina inclined toward multiplicity based on differences in contrariety, while the majority insisted on unity. When you face a complex issue—like differing opinions in your community or family—do you instinctively multiply distinctions or seek an underlying unity? Which approach brings you closer to truth, and which might be a defense mechanism? This week, pick one disagreement you are part of and try to see if there is a single principle that could resolve it.
?
The majority argued that 'difference of perceived objects does not necessitate difference of perceptions.' In your own life, you often react differently to different people or situations. Do you assume that each reaction requires a different 'faculty' in your soul, or can the same heart feel compassion, anger, and patience? When you feel inner conflict, do you believe your soul is fragmented—or is there one core self that perceives and judges? Tonight, reflect on a moment you were torn between two reactions and ask: Was it really two different 'me's, or one 'me' struggling?
?
Ibn Sina recorded in his Canon that most investigators held the single-sense view, even though he personally inclined toward multiplicity. He did not suppress the evidence. Are you honest about data that contradicts your preferred opinion? When you study a topic in your faith or in life, do you seek out the majority view even if it challenges your own, or do you only collect evidence that supports your inclination? This week, intentionally read one article or watch one lecture from a perspective you normally disagree with—and sit with the discomfort before forming a response.

Refuting the Sense of Touch in Celestial Bodies and Simple Elements
Taftazani refutes the attribution of touch to celestial bodies and elemental simples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Taftazani refutes the attribution of the sense of touch to celestial bodies and elemental simples, and the arguments he uses.

Core Idea
Sensation is not a necessary accompaniment of life.

In this passage, Taftazani tackles the question of whether celestial bodies and simple elements like earth and fire possess the sense of touch. Some argued that because these entities have life (celestial spheres due to psychic motions, elements due to apparent attraction/repulsion), they must have touch. Taftazani refutes both arguments, showing that touch is not an essential property of life, and that the elemental argument is very weak.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Taftazani begins with an analogy about bones.
2
Identify the two claims being refuted
The passage presents two claims: that celestial bodies have touch (by some) and that elemental simples have touch (by others). Locate these claims in the text.
3
Understand the analogies and arguments
Taftazani uses the analogy of bones to show why touch would be detrimental. Then he presents the supporters' arguments for celestial and elemental bodies. Analyse each part.
The celestial argument relies on life due to psychic motions.
4
Grasp the refutation
Focus on Taftazani's refutation: he denies that touch is a necessary concomitant of life absolutely, and dismisses the elemental argument as extremely weak.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومصب (۱) ومصعد للمواد، فيتأذى بذلك، وكالعظام فإنها أساس البدن، ودعامة الحركات، بمعنى أنها تجعل الحركات أشد ، بجعل أعضائها أقوى، فلو أحست. لتألمت بالضغط والمزاحمة (۲)، وبما يرد عليه من المصاكات.
[قال (وأثبتها) :
بعضهم ،للفلكيات، وبعضهم للبسائط العنصرية ] .
أي القوة اللامسة بعضهم للفلكيات زعماً منهم أنها من توابع الحياة، وللأفلاك حياة، لكون حركاتها نفسانية، فيكون لها شعور ولمس بالضرورة. والقول بأنها إنما تكون بجذب الملائم، ودفع المنافي، فيكون وجودها في الفلك الممتنع عليه الكون والفساد ،معطلاً مردود بأن ذلك. إنما هو في الأرضيات، وأما في الفلكيات فيجوز أن توجد لغرض آخر كتلذذها بالملامسة والاصطكاك . والجواب : منع كونها من لوازم الحياة على الإطلاق. وأما ما ذهب إليه البعض من الملامسة للعنصريات بناء على أن الأرض تهوى (۳) من العلو إلى السفل على نهج واحد ، والنار بالعكس، وذلك يدل على شعورهما بالملائم وغير الملائم ففي غاية الضعف.
And [like] a spout and an ascender for materials, so it would be harmed by that; and like bones, for they are the foundation of the body and the support of movements, meaning that they make movements stronger by making its limbs stronger. If they sensed, they would be pained by pressure and jostling and by what comes upon them from collisions. [He said: And some proved it for celestial bodies, and some for elemental simples.] That is, the sense of touch: some [proved it] for celestial bodies, claiming that it is a necessary consequence of life, and the celestial spheres have life because their motions are psychic, so they necessarily have sensation and touch. And the statement that it [the sense of touch] only exists for attracting the agreeable and repelling the harmful, so its existence in the celestial sphere, which is impossible to become and decay, is futile – it is refuted by [the fact] that this [explanation] is only concerning terrestrial things. As for celestial things, it is possible that it exists for another purpose, such as their enjoyment of touching and collision. The answer: preventing that it is a necessary concomitant of life in an absolute sense. As for what some held of [the sense of] touch for elemental simples, based on the fact that earth falls from above to below in one manner, and fire the opposite, and that indicates their feeling of the agreeable and disagreeable – it is extremely weak.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that celestial bodies and elemental simples possess the sense of touch. The author begins with an analogy: if bones (which are insensitive) could feel, they would be pained by their function. Then he presents the argument of those who attribute touch to celestial bodies (based on their having life due to psychic motions) and to elemental simples (based on the perceived attraction and repulsion of earth and fire). He refutes the celestial argument by denying that touch is a necessary concomitant of life, and the elemental argument by dismissing it as extremely weak.

البسائط العنصرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Celestial bodies have sensation like humans because they have life.
Correction: Taftazani argues that touch is not a necessary concomitant of life; life does not automatically imply sensation.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the claim that celestial bodies have the sense of touch?
A) Because they do not have life.
B) Because touch is not a necessary concomitant of life.
C) Because they have no limbs.
D) Because their motions are not psychic.

Ibn Sina's Argument for Multiple Touch Faculties
Taftazani presents Ibn Sina's view that touch comprises multiple faculties based on opposing pairs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's reasoning that touch comprises multiple faculties based on distinct types of opposition among tangible qualities.

Core Idea
Touch is not one sense but a family of senses, each specialized in an opposition.

The sense of touch seems unitary, but Ibn Sina argued otherwise. He noticed that different tangible qualities involve fundamentally different kinds of opposition—like hot versus cold and wet versus dry—which cannot be reduced to a single sensation. This lesson presents his argument, along with the counterview, and introduces his analogy that illustrates why we mistakenly think touch is one sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalysis, then read the full English translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice that the passage first states the majority view before giving Ibn Sina's opinion.
2
Identify the competing views
Locate the majority view that touch is a single power, and then Ibn Sina's view that it is multiple. Mark how each is justified.
The majority uses the principle that different perceptibles do not require different perceptions.
3
Examine Ibn Sina's reasoning and analogy
Ibn Sina argues that distinct kinds of opposition demand distinct faculties. He supports this with an analogy: if taste and touch were both spread over the whole body, they would be mistaken for one sense.
This analogy shows how a common organ can create an illusion of unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ومال) ابن سينا إلى تعددها) :
حسب تعدد التضاد بين الملموسات، فإن بين الحرارة والبرودة نوعاً من
التضاد، غير الذي بين الرطوبة واليبوسة مثلاً، بخلاف تضاد (٤) الطعوم.
الجمهور على أن اللامسة قوة واحدة بها تدرك جميع الملموسات، كسائر الحواس، فإن اختلاف المدركات لا يوجب اختلاف الإدراكات، ليستدل بذلك على تعدد مبانيها (٥) ، وذكر ابن سينا في القانون أن أكثر المحصلين على أن للمس قوي كثيرة، بل قوى أربع. وقال في الشفاء : يشبه أن تكون اللامسة عند قوم، لا نوعاً أخيراً ، بل جنساً لقوى أربع ، أو فوقها منبثاً(۱) معاً في الجلد كله، إحداها حاكمة (٢) في التضاد الذي بين الحار والبارد، والثانية في التضاد، الذي بين الرطب واليابس والثالثة في الذي بين الصلب واللين والرابعة في الذي بين الخشن ،والأملس ، إلا أن اجتماعها في آلة واحدة يوهم تأحدها في الذات (۳). :وقال أيضاً. يشبه أن تكون قوى اللمس قوى كثيرة، تختص كل واحدة منها بمضادة، فيكون ما يدرك به المضادة التي بين الثقيل (٤) والخفيف ، غير ما يدرك به المضادة التي بين الحار والبارد . فإن هذه أفعال أولية للحس، يجب أن يكون لكل جنس منها قوة خاصة، إلا أن هذه القوى لما انتشرت في جميع الالات بالسوية، ظنت قوة واحدة، كما لو كان اللمس والذوق منتشرين في البدن كله انتشارهما في اللسان ، لظن مبدؤهما قوة واحدة، فلما تميزا عرف اختلافهما، وليس يجب أن يكون لكل واحدة من هذه القوى آلة تخصها ، بل يجوز أن تكون آلة واحدة مشتركة .لها. ويجوز أن يكون هناك انقسام في الآلات غير محسوس .
He said: And Ibn Sina inclined to its multiplicity according to the multiplicity of opposition among tangible things; for between heat and cold there is a kind of opposition different from that between wetness and dryness, for example, unlike the opposition of tastes. The majority [hold] that the sense of touch is a single power by which all tangibles are perceived, like the other senses, for difference of perceptibles does not necessitate difference of perceptions, to infer from that the multiplicity of its foundations. And Ibn Sina mentioned in al-Qanun that most of the investigators [hold] that touch has many powers, indeed four powers. And he said in al-Shifa: It resembles that the sense of touch according to some people is not a lowest species, but a genus for four powers, or above them, diffused together throughout the whole skin; one of them judges concerning the opposition between hot and cold, the second concerning the opposition between wet and dry, the third concerning that between hard and soft, and the fourth concerning that between rough and smooth, except that their coming together in one organ gives the illusion of their unity in essence. And he also said: It resembles that the powers of touch are many powers, each one of them being specialized in one opposition; so that by which the opposition between heavy and light is perceived is other than that by which the opposition between hot and cold is perceived. For these are primary acts of sense; there must be for each genus a special power, except that when these powers are spread uniformly in all organs, they are thought to be one power, just as if touch and taste were spread throughout the whole body as they are spread in the tongue, their principle would be thought one power; when they were distinguished, their difference would be known. And it is not necessary that each of these powers have a particular organ; rather it is permissible that they share one common organ, and it is permissible that there is an imperceptible division in the organs.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's argument for the multiplicity of the sense of touch. First, the author states the majority view that touch is a single power because differing perceptibles do not necessitate differing perceptions. Then Ibn Sina is cited: in al-Qanun he holds that touch has many powers—specifically four. In al-Shifa he elaborates: touch is not a species but a genus of four powers (hot/cold, wet/dry, hard/soft, rough/smooth), which give the illusion of unity because they share one organ (the skin). He gives an analogy: if taste and touch were both diffused throughout the body, they would seem one; only their separation shows they are distinct.

Key Terms
اللامسة
الملموسات
المضادة
القوى الأربع
الآلة المشتركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Touch must be a single sense because it uses one organ (the skin).
Correction: Ibn Sina argues that multiple powers can share a common organ, as shown by the analogy of taste and touch being confused if co-extensive.

Checkpoint
How does Ibn Sina argue that touch is multiple senses?
A) By noting that different types of opposition exist among tangibles.
B) By citing the majority of scholars.
C) By analogy with hearing.
D) By analyzing the structure of the skin.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina argues that the diversity of opposition among tangibles necessitates multiple faculties of touch, which appear unified only because they share a common organ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Sina says the powers are so uniformly spread that they are thought to be one. In what area of your life—perhaps a habit or a relationship—have you assumed unity where there is actually a rich, hidden diversity? How might acknowledging that multiplicity change your approach? Commit to identifying one such area this week.
?
The majority view argues that difference in objects does not necessitate difference in faculties. When have you accepted a comforting, simple explanation over a more complex truth? What might you be losing by not interrogating that assumption? Resolve to examine one belief you hold about yourself or your faith that you have never questioned.
?
If taste and touch were distributed throughout the body, you would not know they are distinct. What does this teach you about the limits of your own perception? How can you actively seek to uncover the hidden layers of your own soul—your motivations, fears, and desires—rather than settling for a surface-level understanding? Name one step you will take today.
